==PAGE:3==
Abstract 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 1 of 102 
25.06.2015 
Abstract 
The cells of the human body are surrounded by a complex system of chemicals, pores, ridges, 
fibres, and other cells. The surroundings provide a variety of cues that can direct and change cell 
behaviour. Cells react to small topographical cues in their environment down to the nanoscale. 
Studies have shown that stylized easy producible and reproducible topological features such as 
grooves, ridges, steps, pores, pillars, wells, and nodes can direct cell behaviour. Research in 
biomaterial aims to find materials that can replace or restore human tissue function. By 
understanding exactly how cells react to distinct topographical cues it might be possible to control 
the cellular response to implantation of biomaterials and through that reduce healing time and 
increase functionality 
This thesis study how topographical gradients in conjunction with image cytometry and high 
throughput automated image analysis software can be used to provide meaningful information on 
cell behaviour. There are three objectives of this thesis. The first objective was to show how image 
cytometry and machine learning can be used to automatically separate co-cultures of endothelial 
cells and fibroblast even when cultured on irregular topographical surfaces. The secondary 
objective was to show the power of using topographical gradients as an examination tool to monitor 
how topography can change the features of endothelial cells and fibroblasts. The third objective was 
to look into some of the challenges that can be encountered in cross field collaborations. In 
particular challenges encountered when collaborators are asked to apply new methods in culturing 
monocytes on nanoscale gradient substrates. 
Using automated segmentation of fluorescence microscopy images and a random forest classifier it 
was possible to separate images of endothelial cells from fibroblasts with an overall error rate of 
16.72%. It was found that the most important features in the classification were features based on 
the texture of the cells actin structure. 
There is little doubt that the nano-pillars influence fibroblast morphology and that a gradient of 
nano-pillars affects the cells on a continuous scale. For the majority of the features presented, it 
could be seen that fibroblasts react almost linearly up to a plateau that starts at around pillar heights 
of 100 nm and end around a pillar height of 200 nm. Endothelial cells seemed to be less affected by 
the topography. 
Several challenges were encountered when collaborators were asked to apply, to them, unfamiliar 
methods in culturing monocytes on nanoscale gradient substrates for image cytometry. Clustering 
of cells, liquid bobbles under the coverslip and heterogonous staining was just some of the issues 
encountered.

Introduction 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 8 of 102 
25.06.2015 
1. Introduction 
Studies of how surface topography of biomaterials affects cell behaviour have shown that this is a 
field of massive potential for the biomedical industry 1. Historically, the science of biomaterials has 
focused on biological inert materials to avoid immune response 2. More recently the focus has 
changed towards using biomaterials to manipulate cell behaviour to obtain a desired effect. This 
take advantage of the abilities of living cells to reduce inflammation and improve healing which 
increase implanted biomaterial reliability and function. Many research groups have studied how 
cells might change behaviour when exposed to chemicals and/or drugs. However, chemicals and 
drugs can have unwanted systemic side effects. Studies of how biomaterial surfaces influence cells 
have become an area of great interest, because the surface of biomaterials influence cells through 
contact1. Thereby, the effects are local and unwanted systemic side effects can be avoided.  
Studies of biomaterial surfaces have spanned from investigations of modifying surface topography 
or  surface chemistry to varying surface charge 3. In 1911 Harrison grew cells on spider webs4, and 
made it clear that surface topography of the extracellular environment can influence cell behaviour. 
Since then, it has been determined that topographical features down to nanoscale has an impact on 
cell attachment, morphology, proliferation, spreading, cell differentiation, gene expression, and 
consequently cell fate 1,5–9 
Early uses of topography in biomaterials focused on known industrial procedures such as 
sandblasting, chemical etching, or chemical surface treatment. Their main focus was on corrosion 
resistance, stiffness, and implant retention. However, this resulted in less definable surface 
characteristics6 and their effect on cell behaviour was not a major concern at the time. Brunette et 
al.10 suggested that future studies in designing biomaterials should focus on applying cellular 
biology and thereby let the knowledge of cell behaviour guide the research. Therefore, more recent 
studies focus on systematically investigating the effects of different topographical features such as 
ridges, grooves, pillars, and holes 6,7. These studies investigate how varying the height, depth and 
spacing of topographical features influence cell behaviour. As is summarized in a number of 
reviews  most of these experiments focus on the investigation of a few samples where one or two 
parameters are varied at a few discrete levels6,7. This can provide useful information on how cells 
react, but they are dependent on a sometimes arbitrary choice of the few samples selected by the 
investigator. Using samples with only one topography have the consequence that a new sample 
substrate has to be created each time a new set of feature sizes has to be examined. Afterwards, the 
examiner must carefully repeat the same setup for all substrates to make results comparable. 
==PAGE:11==
Introduction 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 9 of 102 
25.06.2015 
In this thesis cells cultured on gradients of topographical features are used to study how nanoscale 
changes affect cell behaviour. A continuous change in surface topography, a gradient, such as 
increasing pillar height or increasing groove distance, will allow for investigation of how cells react 
to the entire range of topographical features on one sample. This will allow the investigator to 
monitor subtle changes in cell behaviour along the gradient, and allow the cells freedom of mobility 
to mover to a preferable surface. Furthermore, this could provide a basis for finding optimal surface 
features that elicit a specific cell reaction on a continuous scale. With modern nanoscale production 
techniques gradients are easily producible and one experimental setting can examine the effects of a 
range of feature sizes. 
Many studies in cell behaviour today focus on monitoring gene expression and/or biomarkers. This 
remains the gold standard for classification of the type, phenotype, and activity level of cell cultures 
as a whole. However, it limits investigating subculture variations within cultures. Furthermore, it 
has no spatial resolution that would be needed to monitor changes along gradients. Microscopy has 
become a major area of interest in order to establish how cells are affected by topography. 
Microscopy has the advantage of enabling the direct observation of cell changes on a single cell 
level even in real time. However, the use of microscopy often presents a bottle neck as it relies on 
manually measured attributes or expert interpretation by physicians or cell biologists11. Therefore, 
many studies only include a limited number of cells to visually investigate. Furthermore, the results 
of the analysis are often limited to a few qualitative statements and a few readily measurable cell 
traits such as area and perimeter11,12. To circumvent this limitation automated image cell analysis, 
i.e. image cytometry, has become an increasingly used method to allow the analysis of large data 
sets of microscopy cell images. In the span of hours, cytometry has the potential to segment 
hundreds of images of thousands of cells, and generate hundreds of quantifiable morphological and 
textural features of cells as a whole or their organelle subparts. These have been shown to 
accurately portray many aspects of cells that are unique to a cell type, unique to a cell phenotype, or 
even unique to cell activity level11–14. 
The identification of cell types and cellular phenotypes often calls for labelling cell specific markers 
that only provides a yes or no answer to whether the markers are present. This can be a laborious 
process as each marker needs to be labelled individually. In this thesis label-free identification use 
utilized. In label-free analysis structures common for all cells are marked and the differences in 
those structures are used to identify the cells. Thereby, the cells can be segmented and quantifiable 
measures of their state can be attained. 
Image cytometry generates massive amounts of data. For example, 200 images captured with a 10x 
objective can easily result in information on more than 10,000 cells from which numerous features 
==PAGE:12==
Introduction 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 10 of 102 
25.06.2015 
can be extracted from each. These features can be obtained from the cell as a whole or from patterns 
of cytoplasmic or membranous organelles and receptors potentially resulting in thousands of 
features describing each cell. Luckily, modern advances in machine learning have resulted in 
procedures that can analyse large amount of data within a relatively short time frame. 
This thesis will study three different cell types. These are endothelial cells, fibroblast and 
monocytes. All of which are essential in the response of the body to implantation of biomaterials.  
This thesis study how topographical gradients in conjunction with image cytometry and high 
throughput automated image analysis software can be used to provide meaningful information on 
cell behaviour. There are three objectives of this thesis. The first objective was to show how image 
cytometry and machine learning can be used to automatically separate co-cultures of endothelial 
cells and fibroblast even when cultured on irregular topographical surfaces. In particular to study if 
this combination can be used to perform label-free segmentation of the cell types and which cell 
traits that differentiate the two cell types can be determined. The second objective was to show the 
power of using topographical gradients as an examination tool to monitor how topography can 
change the features of endothelial cells and fibroblasts on a continuous scale. The third objective 
was to look into some of the challenges that can be encountered in cross field collaborations. In 
particular challenges encountered when collaborators are asked to apply new methods in culturing 
monocytes on nanoscale gradient substrates. The purpose of the monocyte nanoscale gradient 
experiments was to establish if distinction between monocyte phenotypes were using label-free 
identification was possible and to quantify the traits that differentiate the phenotypes.  
The thesis will start by providing theoretical foundation for the understanding of how topography 
influences cell behaviour and fate. This will be done by first introducing cell characteristics in 
general followed by some of the characteristics that are particular for the three types of cells used in 
this thesis. This will provide the reader an understanding why investigations into monocytes, 
fibroblast and endothelial cells are important in biomaterial design. This is followed by a short 
section explaining how cells and topography interact. This thesis is continued by theoretical 
explanation of the production of topographies, how cells are imaged, and finally how segmentation 
and data analysis can be performed using open source image analysis tools. The theoretical 
foundation is, then followed by explanation of the methods and materials used in this thesis. The 
results and discussion chapter is divided into four sections: a) Automated label-free co-culture 
separation of endothelial cells and fibroblasts; b) Nano-pillar gradient as a tool for co-culture 
analysis; c) The impact of the topographical gradient on endothelial and fibroblast co-culture 
behaviour; and d) Challenges in processing monocyte samples for automated label-free phenotype 
separation. 
 
==PAGE:13==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 11 of 102 
25.06.2015 
2. Theory 
2.1 Cells 
The eukaryotic cell is a complex biological machine that serves very specific purposes from the 
force generation of myocytes to the phagocytic ability of the macrophages. A schematic drawing of 
a cell is shown in Figure 2.1.  
 
Figure 2.1 Schematic drawing of a cell and its compartments15 
The outermost part of a cell consists of a phospholipid bilayer plasma membrane. The plasma 
membrane is filled with proteins that function as signal transducers, adhesion molecules, transport 
channels and cell identification markers. Within the cell is the cytoplasm filled with actin 
cytoskeleton, organelles, signalling molecules, proteins, ions and enzymes. The actin cytoskeleton 
is a network of filaments providing the structural scaffold of the cell. Thus, the skeleton is 
responsible for shape, movement, and contraction of the cell. Furthermore, it anchors the organelles 
and provides pathways for signalling. The organelles are small protein machines that provide 
specific function within the cell. The most important organelle is the cell nucleus containing the cell 
DNA and thus provides the basis for the cell function and replication.  
In the following pages three cell types investigated in this thesis will be presented: endothelial cells, 
fibroblasts, and monocytes.  
2.1.1 Endothelial Cells 
The endothelial cell lines the inside of the cardiovascular and lymphatic system and form a 
monolayer of tightly joined cells called the endothelium. The endothelium protects the vessel wall 
from inflammatory cell infiltrates, thrombus formation and smooth muscle cell proliferation16. The 
typical shape of an endothelia cell view in fluorescent microscopy is seen in Figure 2.2. 
==PAGE:14==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 12 of 102 
25.06.2015 
 
 
Figure 2.2 10x fluorescence microscopy image of endothelia cell. Red is actin. Blue is nuclei. 
The interest in endothelial cell in regard to biomaterials is caused by their effects on stents, heart 
valves and other cardiovascular implants. Balloon angioplasty and stenting result in severe damage 
to the endothelium at the site of treatment. This initiates a complex cascade of inflammatory 
processes that may lead to the development of in-stent restenosis17. Similarly, vascular 
interventions, such as percutaneous coronary intervention or bypass grafting, cause accelerated 
endothelial dysfunction, atherosclerosis and restenosis16. The lack of endothelial cell in prosthetic 
vascular grafts is a strong stimulus for intimal hyperplasia16. It have been shown that stent 
thrombosis can be prevented and the restenotic process reduced by coating the stent with antibodies 
from endothelia progenitors prior to inserting the stent18. However, this method has been questioned 
due to the ability for endothelial progenitors to change into smooth muscle cells and increase 
hyperplasia16. Therefore, other opportunities are being studied as endothelial cells have been found 
to be highly responding to changes in topography and shear stress 19–22. Endothelial cells move and 
proliferate until the density of endothelial cells reaches a level at which cell-to-cell contact between 
three or more endothelial cells halts proliferation23. It has been found that strength of these cell-to-
cell junctions formed by endothelial cells can be diminished and that proliferation and migration 
can be increased by microscale fibrous topographies 24. Individual endothelial cells seeded on 
grooves and fibres down to the nanoscale have been found to align their orientation and actin 
cytoskeleton 22,25. Understanding endothelial cell repopulation post-stenting and how this modulates 
in-stent restenosis is critical to improving arterial healing post-stenting. 
2.1.2 Fibroblasts 
Fibroblast are the active phenotype of fibrocytes. They are responsible for the production and 
maintenance of the extracellular matrix (ECM), a network of collagen, hyoloronic acid, 
proteglycans, laminis, fibronectin6, and other collagen structures and are vital in wound healing. 
The fibroblasts influence the structure and characteristics of the ECM through organization of 
collagen fibrils26. Fibroblasts are very heterogeneous in morphology as their morphology is highly 
==PAGE:15==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 13 of 102 
25.06.2015 
dependent on their activity and location. Fibroblast are usually characterised by elongated irregular 
shape with convex, concave parts as well as protrusions27, as seen in Figure 2.3. Fibroblasts are 
often scattered but usually not isolated as they adhere to neighbouring fibroblasts through 
protrusions27.  
 
 
Figure 2.3 10x fluorescence microscopy image of fibroblasts. Red is actin. Blue is nuclei. 
The interest in fibroblasts in biomaterials is caused by their ability to be recruited by the immune 
system and thereby potentially encapsulate biomaterial in a fibrous capsule2 leading to lack of 
implant retention and function. The severity depends on how long and severe the inflammation 
following the implantation is. Designing surfaces that inactivate or force fibroblasts to move away 
from the biomaterial might decrease the level of fibrous capsule formation to increase implant 
retention and function. Fibroblasts are very receptive to changes in topography. Studies have found 
that fibroblast oriented along and conform to microgroove structures 28. Furthermore, changes in 
degree of attachment, shape, size and perimeter of fibroblasts exposed to pillars, grooves, and pit 
topography have been observed 29–31. Furthermore, it has been found that topography and affects 
how fibroblasts add new fibres to the ECM 5.  
2.1.3 Monocytes 
Macrophages are white blood cells responsible for the phagocytosis of pathogens and damaged 
tissue. They work as antigen presenting cells through which they activate other parts of the immune 
system. Furthermore, depending on their phenotype macrophages release pro- or anti-inflammatory 
cytokines that modulate the immune response2. Macrophages reside beneath the free surfaces of the 
body such as skin, mucous membranes, and blood vessels32. In Figure 2.4 a number of typical 
monocyte can be seen. 
 
==PAGE:16==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 14 of 102 
25.06.2015 
 
Figure 2.4 40x fluorescence microscopy image of monocytes. Red is actin. Blue is nuclei. 
Macrophages have been objects of special interest in investigations of biomaterials because of their 
role in regeneration of tissue. Prolonged inflammation time can cause granuloma and fibrous 
formation and thereby reduce implant retention. These effects are especially detrimental for 
regenerative biomaterials where the goal is to replace lost tissue and avoid scar tissue formation2.  
It has been hypothesized that the ratio of pro-inflammatory to anti-inflammatory macrophages are 
important in healing33. It is believed that macrophages undergo distinct phenotypic changes on 
biomaterials surfaces over time. All macrophages, regardless of cell origin or culture surface, shift 
toward pro-inflammatory phenotypes in vivo during the response to a foreign body 34. 
Macrophages have a fluent continuum of phenotypes stretching from the pro-inflammatory in the 
one end to the anti-inflammatory in the other35. Along the continuum macrophages present different 
markers and release different cytokines at different levels. Macrophage phenotypes are often 
distinguished by this differential expression of surface markers 34,36. However, few, if any, known 
marker combinations can definitively segregate the continuum of different phenotypes36. Therefore, 
the focus relies mainly on markers known for the extremes.  
The pro-inflammatory (M1) phenotypes of macrophages emerge as a result of pro-inflammatory 
cytokines such as interferon-g2,34 and they produce calprotectin that can be used as a marker for M1. 
The anti-inflammatory (M2) phenotype of macrophages is the result of activation by cytokines 
released from other immune cells and such as IL-4 2,34 an they express mannose receptors that can 
be used as a marker for M2.  
Macrophages are highly heterogeneous and can rapidly, change function in response to local 
environment36. Several studies have shown that surfaces with different heights of groves can 
increase or change inflammatory activity and/or change the morphology of macrophages33,35,37,38. 
2.2 Topography  
The cells of the human body reside in a complex three-dimensional environment of pores, ridges, 
fibres, minerals, ions, chemical molecules, and other cells to which cells can adhere, interact and 
==PAGE:17==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 15 of 102 
25.06.2015 
function26. Cells rest on the ECM that has been found to differ in topography in specialized tissues 
around the body6. All parts of the extracellular environment can influence cell behaviour. Contact 
adhesion with the extracellular matrix of pores, ridges, fibres and minerals can change the 
morphology and subsequent behaviour of a cell6.  
Many molecular aspects of cell reaction to topography are still being debated. However, it is 
recognised that topography influence cell behaviour through contact adhesion1. In the cell 
membrane are located a wide range of transmembrane adhesion molecules responsible for cells 
attachment to the ECM and other cells. The cells constantly extend and retract small protrusions of 
cell membrane to sense their environment. If adhesion molecules in the protrusions attaches to the 
ECM they are activated. The pattern of the activated adhesion molecules depends on the pattern of 
available attachment points. On the inside of the cell a plethora of different signalling molecules are 
gathering and attaching to the activated adhesion molecules forming an adhesion complex. The 
aggregation of molecules forms a self-reinforcing signal that promote further aggregation 39, 
resulting in focal complexes 30-100nm in size consisting of hundreds of molecules 40. In the 
adhesion complex molecules that are not usually found in the vicinity of each other are forced 
together. The pattern of adhesion points greatly influence the structure of the adhesion complex and 
thereby how, when and which signalling pathways are activated or suppressed39.  
The adhesion itself is driven by actin polymerization 40. The actin cytoskeleton is highly dependent 
on where adhesion complexes form in the cell, how strong they are and what signalling pathways 
they activate 1,39. The actin cytoskeleton is highly dependent on the distance between and individual 
strength of adhesion complexes. As an example two weak adhesion complexes in vicinity can be 
strengthened by the actin cytoskeleton building a bridge between adhesions40. However, if the 
distance is too great no bridge is formed and adhesion might fail. It has been shown that the 
geometry of potential adhesion points and the distance between them control both gene expression 
and cell morphology 8,41. 
The material strength of the ECM also affects cell behaviour. The force applied by actin contraction 
through adhesions or by the ECM through adhesions are a prerequisite for adhesion formation and 
actin polymerization 42,43. In the early stages of focal complex formation the focal adhesions react to 
the stress induced by the actin polymerization when the cytoskeleton remodels42. Several proteins 
needed in actin polymerization are activated by pulling forces 44. Forces are necessary to activate 
part of the signalling pathway of adhesions. Furthermore, forces have major impact on the size of 
the adhesion complex and thereby the number of potential signalling molecules involved in the 
adhesion. External forces applied to cells are equally important, as increased shear leads to increase 
size of focal adhesions20.  
==PAGE:18==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 16 of 102 
25.06.2015 
Adhesion complexes become centres of local and global control of the cell. 40. Focal adhesions 
function as a two way signalling pathway where input from ECM and output from cell is 
conveyed45. Cells sense the mechanics and topography of the ECM through activation and 
suppression of signalling pathways and through reorganization of the actin cytoskeleton. In 
response cells can influence the ECM by releasing cytokines, laying out new ECM or applying 
force through actin contraction.  
Through adhesion, cell contact with the ECM has been found to be essential cell to growth, 
regulation, differentiation, proliferation, and survival1,5. However, mapping how the extracellular 
environment influence the individual parts of cell behaviour is no easy task. Cell-to-cell and cell-to-
matrix adhesion can have opposite effects on proliferation and growth39. In addition it has been 
found that findings in one cell line are not necessarily transferable to other cell lines39. It has been 
suggested that even though all the components and their function should one day be identified, the 
problem with integrating all the individual effects into their resulting effects as a complex might 
show to be in unsurpassable as extensive crosstalk between components of the adhesion complex 
exist1. Predicting cell behaviour to certain topographical features is difficult as there seem to be no 
common trend between various cell types and cell adhesion. On some surfaces fibroblast has seen 
to decrease adhesion while muscle cells increase adhesion7.  
2.2.1 Topographical substrate production  
It has been hypothesized that specialized functions may be enhanced by topographies similar to the 
topology of the ECM6. Brunette et al10 suggested that future studies in designing biomaterials 
should focus on using knowledge of cell behaviour to guide the research. Attempts have been made 
to copy the topography of the ECM in order to facilitate examination of cell behaviour in contact 
with their own negative signature46. However, since a single chemical group, a molecular 
conformation ,or folding of an ECM structure can make a distinct topographical feature it is 
difficult to isolate the underlying feature responsible for cellular interaction with ECM1.Therefore, 
the majority of studies have been focusing on the effects of stylized easy producible and 
reproducible topological features such as grooves, ridges, steps, pores, pillars, wells, and nodes6.  
Many of the biomaterial surfaces today are designed on computers and transferred to substrates 
using lithography, laser deposition or etching7. Lithography is a widely used technique to produce 
topographic features of micro- and nanoscale. In lithography a substrate is treated with a resist 
which is a thin layer of photosensitive material, the process is show in Figure 2.5. The resist is then 
irradiated with light or an electron beam through a mask causing the irradiated resist to become 
either more or less soluble than the remainder of the resist. A solvent called the developer is then 
==PAGE:19==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 17 of 102 
25.06.2015 
applied to the surface. After exposure to the developer either a positive or negative of the irradiated 
mask will be present on the surface of the substrate. By using electron beam lithography patterns 
with features down to a few nm in size can be produced47. 
 
 
Figure 2.5 Example of how a lithography is produced48. 
2.2.2 Injection Moulding 
While lithography is a well-established technique in the microelectronics industry47 the large scale 
production of topographies can be achieved by injection moulding. In the injection moulding 
process polymer pellets are placed in a hopper from where the pellets are fed into a heated barrel, 
Figure 2.6. A tapered screw turns and pushes the pellets forward, towards a nozzle. As the pellets 
are pushed forward they are heated above the glass transition temperature as a result of the heated 
barrel and the shear between pellets and the turning screw. The result is that the polymer is readily 
able to flow in the injection chamber in front of the screw. By hydraulic or electrical actuation, the 
screw is pushed forward and the molten polymer is injected into the sealed cavity of a tool after 
which the screw is extracted. After a cooling period the mould is opened and the moulded part is 
released. In figure 3.1 is seen a graphical representation of the injection moulding system. 
 
==PAGE:20==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 18 of 102 
25.06.2015 
 
Figure 2.6 Schematic representation of injection moulding. Modified from Reynolds49 
In order to allow the same tool to be used for numerous topographies an inlay which has the 
negative of the wanted features can be produced. At first a master of the topography is produced by 
photolithography. A negative of the master is then imprinted on polymer plate using pressure and 
heat. This negative imprint is used as an inlay in the tool for the injection moulding and ensures that 
moulded parts are imprinted with topographical features. Several thousand of nano- and micro-
patterned samples have been produced at the University of Glasgow using this method without 
visible degradation of the inlays. 
2.3 Cell Imaging 
Cells are imaged in a magnitude of ways from ordinary light microscopy of colour stained samples 
to imaging of individual molecules using scanning electron microscopes. Proper investigation of 
large sets of cell samples requires the ability to automate the imaging and analysis of large quantity 
of cell samples to avoid the slow manual analysis process. 
Automated fluorescence microscopy allows unattended acquisition of cellular images across several 
samples even in real time. Fluorescence microscopy is a very common method to visualise cells50. 
In fluorescence microscopy one sample can be marked by different fluorescent stains that bind to 
different cellular structures and emits light at specific wave lengths that can be observed on separate 
channels by the microscope detector.  
The most commonly used type of fluorescence microscopy is the epifluorescence microscope, 
which is shown in Figure 2.7. Epifluorescence microscopes function by a light source emitting an 
excitation light at a specific wave length. The lights are directed by a mirror and focused through an 
objective before hitting the sample. As the light hits the fluorescent markers on the sample most of 
the light passes through. The remaining light excites the fluorescent stains that emit light at a higher 
wave length. The emitted light and a small amount of reflected excitation light passes back through 
==PAGE:21==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 19 of 102 
25.06.2015 
the objective and through a wavelength specific filter to remove the remaining excitation light and 
is registered on a detector or observed through and ocular.  
 
 
Figure 2.7 Schematic drawing of fluorescence microscope51 
In many automated fluorescence microscopes the detector is a camera. The emission light from 
different stains that each represents specific cell organelles or markers can be visualized on 
individual fluorescence images and easily be separated and matched in later image analysis.  
Before imaging of cells using fluorescence microscopy it is crucial to make the cells fluorescent in 
a way that retains the morphology and structure of the cell. In addition, the fluorescent stain needs 
to be specific and sensitive to structures or molecules of interest. In general there are three methods 
utilized to stain with fluorescent markers. First, some molecules have an intrinsic fluorescence and 
bind to a specific biological molecule. An example of this type of molecule is DAPI (4’,6-
Diamidino-2-Phenylindole) that binds to the DNA of cells and thereby becomes a very popular 
stain for cell nuclei. Secondly, some drugs and toxins bind to specific structures. By attaching a 
fluorescent marker (fluorophore or fluorochrome) to these drugs or toxins the structures can be 
stained. Phalloidin is an example of very popular molecule of this type used to stain actin and can 
be linked to a number of fluorescent markers that emit light at different wave lengths. Finally, 
immunofluorescence takes advantage of the natural antibody antigen interaction. Antibodies 
function by binding to antigens and the immune system produces antibodies to exactly match a 
specific antigen. Thereby, antibodies bind to very specific structures. By identifying or creating 
antibodies that bind to antigens that a specific to particular parts of cells it is possible to have very 
specific fluorescent markers.  
==PAGE:22==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 20 of 102 
25.06.2015 
2.4 Image Processing 
The second step in the automation process is image processing. Image processing is necessary in 
order to extract the information available in the images captures by microscope. The image 
processing usually consists of 3 steps: i) image preparation; ii) image segmentation; and iii) cell 
feature extraction.  
Image processing is the initial management of an image. In this step it might be needed to correct 
illumination of the image, enhance the edges, resize, or perform other image processing steps50. 
This can be used to increase homogeneity between samples, decrease background noise, and 
enhance image features before the segmentation. Segmentation is the process of identifying objects 
within an image. Many different approaches to cell segmentation have been proposed. The main 
concern of all segmentation algorithms is consistency and accuracy11. If not segmented equally 
across the hundreds of images that can be involved in automated image analysis there will be no 
foundation for comparison. Therefore, there is a demand for a robust algorithm that can handle 
touching and overlapping cells as well as large fluctuations in stain intensity, due to low 
representation of the stained molecule or uneven staining. 
The need for initial image processing depends largely on the quality of the captured images and can 
differ greatly from study to study depending on purpose and available image capturing equipment. 
However, the principles of segmentation of cells remain constant. While, there are many methods to 
segment objects in images this thesis will focus on the workflow of the automated open source cell 
segmentation software CellProfiler (Broad Institute)11. CellProfiler has several advantages. Many 
peer-reviewed published segmentation algorithms specific for cell, yeast and worm cultures are 
implemented in CellProfiler, and users can add additional by writing them in python. In addition 
CellProfiler can identify objects in an image of one fluorescence channel and use the information in 
another channel. CellProfiler has a graphical user interface and works in a pipeline structure with 
modules. Each module performs a specific task, can easily be added or removed and will be 
executed in the order in which they are arranged.  
A typical pipeline starts by adding an image library. The system is then set up to extract the wanted 
information from the metadata available from the image file information and title. Next step is 
segmentation starting with identification of primary objects. Primary objects are preferably cell 
structures that are easily segmented and can be used as a reference point for further segmentation. A 
typical example would be a DAPI stained cell nucleus. The nucleus is a good reference point for a 
cell. Next is the identification of secondary objects. Secondary objects are identified in relation to a 
primary object and it is assumed that a primary object will lie within a secondary object. This could 
==PAGE:23==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 21 of 102 
25.06.2015 
be finding the actin structure of the cell belonging to the found cell nucleus. The last step of the 
segmentation is the localization of tertiary objects. Tertiary objects lie within secondary objects and 
outside primary objects. Thereby tertiary object could be cytoplasmic components of a cell such as 
organelles. In Figure 2.8 can be seen an example of the identification of primary and secondary 
objects. 
 
Figure 2.8 An example of the segmentation process. (A) The channel with the cell nuclei (black). (B) Cell nuclei edge has been 
identified (pink). (C) Cell nuclei outline (pink) used as centre of cell and cell outline determined(black). (D) Al layers with 
outline of cell. Black: Outline, Blue: nuclei, Red: Actin stain. (E) Cell touching edges removed.   
2.5 Feature Extraction 
Final step in automated image analysis is feature extraction. Feature extraction from images of cells 
is the measurement of image moments and has proven to be a great utility in automated analysis of 
cellular images 52. The purpose of extracting image moments of the cells is to convert the 
potentially infinite number of pixels in an image into a finite set of features that adequately 
represents the information available in the image. The primary interest of feature extraction should 
be to determine how an image may be classified by a small number of moments 53. Hundreds of 
different image moments have been suggested. From the simple central moments such as area, 
perimeters and eccentricity to less easily interpretable features based on image decomposition or 
frequency analysis. 
The CellProfiler software has several modules dedicated to extracting features from segmented 
objects. The methods applied in CellProfiler is described on the producers website54. While features 
such as area and shape measurements are easily understood some features used in this thesis might 
warrant further explanation. A small overview of descriptive features is provided in the next pages. 
A complete list of features used in this thesis is available in Appendix A.  
2.5.1 Area and shape 
Area and shape features are the most basic image moments. They are the characteristics that can be 
considered the most obvious geometrical features of a cell such as area, perimeter, eccentricity, 
==PAGE:24==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 22 of 102 
25.06.2015 
orientation etc. They are the moments most commonly observed by manual visual cell analysis and 
therefore many of these are used and reported in studies involving cell morphology. Because the 
features are derived directly from geometric measurements they have the advantage of being easily 
interpretable and have low computational cost. 
2.5.2 Neighbour 
Neighbour features are concerned with how objects relate to each other within the spatial domain of 
a sample. These measurements involve measurements such as distance to neighbours, difference in 
orientation and percent of cells touching. Thereby the neighbour measures also provide easily 
interpretable measurements. The advantages of these measurements are that they explain how the 
objects relate to each other in the case of cell and that they might show if cells favour or avoid 
contact, if they spread out or stay in clusters and if the organize themselves in systematic patterns. 
2.5.3 Intensity 
Intensity features are concerned with pixel intensity. The intensity features calculate summary 
statistics for the pixels in the objects interior and the pixels along the perimeter of objects. In 
addition to summary statistics, it also locates centre of mass for pixel intensity. The advantage of 
using intensity measures on cell images is that it can monitor how a particular structure stained with 
fluorescent marker is upregulated or restructured. In general an increase in intensity can be 
interpreted as an upregulation, while a change in centre of mass can be interpreted as a 
restructuring. However, care must be taken when dealing with intensity measures. They are very 
sensitive to changes in stain intensity and homogenous image illumination. 
2.5.4 Radial distribution 
Radial distribution features measures the intensity distribution from an objects centre to its 
boundary within a specified number of contours from a selected starting point, either the point 
furthest from any edge or a specified point such as a cell nucleus. After identification of starting 
point the object is divided into N number of contours. The contour size is normalized at a per object 
basis. Each contour follows a fraction of the distance from the centre point as seen in Figure 2.9. 
Within each image the pixel intensity is quantified in one of three ways. This provides a total of 3 
measures for each ring. 
 
==PAGE:25==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 23 of 102 
25.06.2015 
 
Figure 2.9: Cells with contours equally spaced from the centre of the cells. Gray: Cells, Red,blue,purple,yellow: contours55 
FracAtD features provide the fraction of total intensity within a given contour.  
MeanFrac features provide the fraction of total intensity within a given contour, normalized by the 
fraction of pixels within that contour. This is done to compensate for the increasing area of the 
rings. 
RadialCV features provide a measure of variation of intensity within a contour. This measure 
differs from the previous in that each contour is divided into 8 slices as seen in Figure 2.10. Within 
a slice the intensity divided by the number of pixels provides eight values one for each slice. The 
final measure is then calculated as the standard deviation of the 8 values divided by the mean of the 
eight values. 
 
Figure 2.10 Schematic representation of ring division into 8 slices. Used in calculation of the RadialCV measure. 
2.5.5 Shape and texture 
Shape and texture features are fundamental properties of objects therefore it is important to have 
effective shape and texture descriptors53. When working with descriptive textural features it is 
important to find features that are invariant of position size and orientation. Many such moments 
have been proposed Fourier descriptors, autoregressive models, circular harmonic expansions56. 
==PAGE:26==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 24 of 102 
25.06.2015 
CellProfiler suggests two sets of shape and texture descriptors. Zernike moments and Haralick  
features. Because these are not as easily interpretable as the proceeding moments and their 
calculation not as straight forward a thorough description will follow.  
2.5.5.1 
Zernike moments 
While the proposed descriptive textural moments described above are invariant of position, size and 
orientation they do not have an orthogonal basis this implies that correlation exist between the 
extracted features and thereby redundant information is present. In addition solving the equations to 
extract moments requires solving systems of coupled equations and because of this linear 
dependence moments will change if higher order terms are added56. 
Zernike features are a type of image moments estimated by the coefficients to Zernike polynomials. 
Different Zernike representations are shown in Figure 2.11. Zernike polynomials are sets of 
polynomials that are all orthogonal to the unit disc. Any periodic function can be decomposed into 
an infinite sum of its sinusoid components as done with a Fourier transform. A Zernike transform 
relies on the same principle as any sufficiently smooth phase field can be decomposed into a sum of 
mutually orthogonal Zernike polynomials. Like with a Fourier transform the coefficients of each 
Zernike polynomial can be estimated by the Zernike transform. By estimating the coefficients of the 
Zernike polynomials the shape and texture of a cell can be described by the coefficients to the 
Zernike polynomials. While the entire texture of the image would take an infinite sum of Zernike 
polynomials to accurately describe each consequent order or polynomials provide less and less 
information. In fact Teh and Chin57 found that higher order Zernike moments captures more details 
but are subsequently also more susceptible to noise. Therefore, a balance must be found that 
includes enough information without including too much noise.  
 
Figure 2.11 Zernike polynomial representations58 
==PAGE:27==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 25 of 102 
25.06.2015 
Zernike moments are based on orthogonal polynomials. Therefore, they do not contain redundant 
information and only require solving of uncoupled equations to estimate moments. This also 
implies that adding higher order terms does not change lower order moments53.  Zernike moments 
are not rotation and scale invariant. However, they can easily be normalized56. A Zernike moment 
merely experience phase shift upon rotation therefore the magnitude of the Zernike moments 
remain constant. In addition, normalizing the first Zernike moments with a constant renders the 
moments size invariant. All coefficients to Zernike moments are complex and both conjugates are 
coefficients to the equations. This provides an additional advantage as making the moments rotation 
invariant by taking the magnitude cuts the number of features in half while providing the same 
shape and textural information. The advantage of using and orthogonal basis is evident as the 
individual contribution from each polynomial is easily separated and that the moments provide a 
basis for easy reconstruction.  
It has been found that Zernike features scored best in redundancy and image representation 
compared to several other textural features57. Zernike feature have previously been successfully 
used in classification of cells 14,59  
2.5.5.2 Haralick features 
Haralick features are measures of texture concerned with the spatial distribution of grey tones in 
local areas. Measures of grey tone spatial dependence has successfully been used on satellite and 
aerial imagery, X-rays, and microscopy. Furthermore it has provided good results in differentiating 
between things as different as cloud types, ground infrastructure types (roads, railways)60, chest X-
rays, colon cancer types61, and cervical cell types60. 
The Haralick features are 14 image moments calculated from the co-occurrence matrix. The co-
occurrence matrix, C, is a symmetric NxN matrix that describes the frequency at which two grey-
scale levels occur as neighbours in the analysed image, where N is the number of greyscale levels. 
Thereby the entry Cij is equal to the number of times a pixel with greyscale level i is next to a pixel 
with greyscale level j separated by a given distance62. An ordinary greyscale image contains 
hundreds or thousands of greyscale levels. The exact number depends on the bit depth of the image 
and the intensity differences of the photographed object. The construction of the co-occurrence 
matrix follows 3 easy steps. First the greyscale intensities of the image to be analysed is quantized 
into N discrete greyscale levels by thresholding. Secondly the image is traversed pixel by pixel 
horizontally, vertically, diagonally, or anti-diagonally, and it is noted in the co-occurrence matrix 
which greyscale levels that occurs together as neighbours between each pixel. Thereby the co-
occurrence matrix becomes a function of angular relationship as well as distance. Finally, the co-
==PAGE:28==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 26 of 102 
25.06.2015 
occurrence matrix is normalized such that the rows sums to one thereby each entry Cij describes the 
probability of greyscale level is next to greyscale level j in the direction traversed. An example of 
the creation of the co-occurrence matrix can be seen in Figure 2.12. 
 
 
Figure 2.12 Creation of Co-occurrence matrix. Grey scale level 1 found next to level 1 transferred to position 1,1 (top red 
arrow). Grey scale level to found next to level 2 twice entered in position 1,2 (two lower red arrows)63 
As there are four different ways to traverse the image it is possible to construct four different co-
occurrence matrices from an image. If only one direction is traversed the matrix is not invariant to 
rotation therefore either four co-occurrence matrixes are constructed or one containing the means of 
the co-occurrences from each direction.  
In total, 14 Haralick features can be extrapolated from the co-occurrence matrix with the intent of 
summarizing the texture of the image. Each of the 14 Haralick feature is a different measure of how 
the greyscale levels change across the image and thereby descriptors of texture. As an example, if 
the most frequent occurrences of greyscale transitions in the co-occurrence matrix are located along 
the diagonal it means that the majority of pixels are located next to pixels of equal or almost equal 
greyscale level therefore the texture must be very homogenous. The 14 features are summarized in 
Appendix A. 
2.6 Machine Learning 
The automated imaging, segmentation, and feature extraction provide massive data sets. 
Fortunately, the advent of modern computers and machine learning techniques provides methods to 
find patterns in extremely large datasets. Computer algorithms that find patterns can be as simple as 
linear regression and decision trees to more advanced method such as support vector machines and 
neural networks.  
==PAGE:29==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 27 of 102 
25.06.2015 
In recent times ensemble predictors have grown in interest due to their superior accuracy to other 
machine learning methods64. Ensemble predictors have the advantage that they can combine weak 
predictors to provide highly accurate classification and regression64,65. In ensemble predictors 
several copies of the same predictor are trained and the final classification or regression is 
determined by majority vote or mean respectively. The success of ensemble predictors is 
determined by the strength of the individual predictor and the independence of the predictors65. The 
stronger and more uncorrelated the individual predictors are the more accurate the ensemble 
predictor will be. Ensemble predictors especially shown to be superior when small perturbations in 
the original data can lead to large changes in prediction error66. 
Many different approaches to ensure reduced correlation between the individual predictors in an 
ensemble have been suggested. Breiman66 suggested bagging of predictors where each predictor is 
constructed by sampling a dataset with replacement (bootstrapping) until a dataset with the same 
size as the original is collected. By bootstrapping some data points will be sampled multiple times 
and some will be left out, on average it will show that approximately 37% of the original dataset is 
left out of the bootstrapped dataset66. By bootstrapping a representation of the distribution of the 
original data set is indirectly achieved. A predictor is now trained using the bootstrapped dataset. 
This is repeated until a sufficient number of predictors have been trained.  
A second approach suggested by Ho67 is the random subspace method where the feature space is 
sampled. Each predictor in the ensemble is trained on a random selected subset of the original 
available features. Thereby each individual predictor will have a somewhat unique set of features 
available to classify or regress the data. This allows the individual predictors to be highly 
uncorrelated as they are based on different features. 
Random forest was first suggested by Breiman65. The very simple nature of the decision tree is 
combined with the increased resistance to perturbation gained from bagging and with the increase 
in un-correlation gained from the random subspace method. Random forest has been shown to 
generate highly accurate predictors that are equal or surpass the predictive capabilities of other 
popular and advanced methods such as adaboost, support vector machine, and neural networks 
when compared to benchmark biomedical data sets64,65.  
The growth of a random forest is visualized in Figure 2.13. A dataset consisting of N observations 
described by M features is firstly sampled with replacement providing T unique sample sets one for 
training of each of the T trees that is to be grown in the forest. Secondly the feature space is 
sampled T times potentially providing a unique subset of features for each of the T trees to be 
==PAGE:30==
Theory 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 28 of 102 
25.06.2015 
grown. Finally, T decision trees are grown one for each sample set and corresponding feature 
subset. The final classification or regression is now the majority vote or mean respectively. 
Around 37% of the observations are not included in the training of a tree. Testing the forest on these 
observations left out of bag has been shown to provide an unbiased estimation of the prediction 
error. This can in most cases negate the need to cross-validation to estimate the efficiency of the 
predictor. The gained in accuracy with ensemble predictors unfortunately is lost in interpretation as 
it is difficult to directly interpret how each observation is classified. However, the random forest 
algorithm can generate a proximity matrix. The proximity matrix notes the frequency at which 
observations end up together in a terminal branch of a tree within the forest. Thereby, it becomes a 
measure of how similar the observations are. This can be used to find clusters in the data and 
potential outliers. Furthermore, a prototype of each class can be constructed by finding the 
observation that has the highest number of observations from the same class within its K nearest 
neighbours and taking the mean of their feature.  
 
Figure 2.13: Schematic diagram of the random Forest algorithm. 
 
 
==PAGE:31==
Method and Materials 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 29 of 102 
25.06.2015 
3. Method and Materials 
3.1 Topography 
All substrates used in this thesis were produced in house at The University of Glasgow by members 
of the BIG research team. 
Micro- and nano-patterned samples as well as flat controls were produced by injection moulding. 
Substrates were produced in polycarbonate (Makrolon OD2015) by injection moulding using a 
topographical inlay to imprint the topographical features into the surface of the substrate upon 
moulding. They were produced on a Victory 28, Engel GmBH. The polycarbonate pellets were 
dried for 3 hours under vacuum at 110°C, the polycarbonate was injected at 280°C and the tool 
temperature was 80°C.  The Injection moulded samples were 24 by 24 mm and 2 mm thick as 
depicted in Figure 3.1. 
 
 
Figure 3.1: Photo (left) and Schematic drawing (right) of patterned injection moulded substrate. The arrows indicated the 
direction of increasing gradient. 
The topographical substrates had a 9 by 9 mm or 10 by 10 mm topographical pattern at the centre of 
the sample. Substrates with four different topographical gradient patterns were produced, nano-
pillars, nano-pits, dual nano-gradient and flat surface for control. The different substrate types and 
their characteristics are summarized in table 3.1.

The nano-pillar and nano-pit gradients are characterized by having increasing feature height along 
one dimension while having constant feature height along the other dimension as visualized in 
Figure 3.2. 
 
 
Figure 3.2 Schematic representation of increasing feature height/depth along substrate topography. Increasing greyscale level 
is equal to increased feature height/depth. Modified from Reynolds49 
The dual gradient is a gradient of grooves. The grooves are oriented along the axis of increasing 
feature depth. It differs from the remaining because the feature depth increases along the direction 
of the groove and the distance between grooves (pitch) changes along a gradient perpendicular to 
the grooves. A 3D model of the dual gradient inlay used for injection moulding of the dual gradient 
substrates is visualised in Figure 3.3.

grooves. Distance between grooves increase along axis perpendicular to grooves. Modified from Reynolds49 
Stretching of the features occur when the inlay is removed from the substrate after injection 
moulding. Therefore, the features will not completely follow the rectilinear gradient of the inlay 
mould. Inspections of nano-pillar and nano-pit substrates measured by the BIG group performed on 
a previous occasion49 are visualized in the Figure 3.4.

3.2 Fibroblast Endothelial Co-culture 
automated label-free co-culture separation of endothelial cells and fibroblasts 
Co-culture experiments were prepared on nano-pillar samples. Within the 9 by 9 mm patterned area 
the pillars were 100 nm in diameter and separated by 300 nm centre-to-centre, with heights varying 
along the gradient from 0-250 nm. Samples were prepared, imaged and segmented by Paul 
Reynolds at the University of Glasgow. The main points of the seeding and staining of cells will be 
explained in the following paragraphs. The complete seeding and staining protocol with can be 
found in Appendix B. 
Immortalized cell lines were obtained from the Centre for Cell Engineering, University of Glasgow 
and were used due to their ease of use and availability. The two cell lines used were human 
fibroblasts (hTERT) and murine lung capillary endothelial (LE2). 
3.2.1 Sample seeding 
Prior to seeding of the two cell populations endothelial cells were incubated with 5µM green 
CellTrackerTM dye (Molecular Probes, UK) as specified by the manufacturer. The tracker dye is a 
membrane permeable non fluorescent dye that after uptake by the cell is cleaved by regular cell 
metabolism and consequently rendered a membrane impermeable fluorescent dye. By only dyeing 
the endothelial cells the tracker dye becomes a marker for endothelial cells that can later be used as 
a control measure in separating endothelial cells from fibroblasts. All excess tracker dye was 
removed from the endothelial population after incubation by washing. This was done to ensure that 
fibroblasts would not internalize excess dye after mixing of the two cell cultures. 
Using the Uniform Cell Seeding Device49, the cells were seeded onto 5 nano-pillar gradient 
substrates at a density of 4000 cells cm-2 and was incubated for a total of 96 hours. 
3.2.2 Staining 
Following the incubation period the samples were fixed in paraformaldehyde, permeabilized with a 
0.1% Triton-X 100TM solution thereafter blocked in Bovine Serum to reduce the chance of 
nonspecific binding. The actin cytoskeleton was stained with rhodamine-phalloidin cytoskeletal 
marker. Samples were mounted with size 0 coverslips using a mounting media containing DAPI. 
Thereby the cell DNA was stained by DAPI. 
3.2.3 Microscopy 
Images of the co-culture samples were acquired using an Olympus CX 41 (Olympus, Japan) 
microscope equipped with 10X objective and a motorized stage. The camera and stage was operated 
using ImageProPlus (Media Cybernetics, UK). Images were acquired as linear scans of 18 
==PAGE:35==
Method and Materials 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 33 of 102 
25.06.2015 
continuous locations along the gradient. The first four images of the scan were captured on the flat 
part of the substrate before the gradient, the next 10 were captured on the nano-pillar gradient, and 
the remaining four were captured on the flat surface following the gradient. A total of 12 scans 
across three samples were captured resulting in 216 images. A schematic overview of the scans 
along a sample can be seen in Figure 3.5. The three fluorescent stains were automatically captured 
on three separate channels at each location. Thereby separate images of DNA, actin, and tracker dye 
were captured. 
Care was taken to properly calibrate and set up the microscope by aligning the light source to 
provide flat field illumination, minimizing background light and the presence of contaminant 
particles. In addition, care was taken to maintain consistent exposure, light and gains levels across 
all images to decrease noise and inter-sample variation. 
 
Figure 3.5 Linear scan lines for capturing continuous locations along substrate gradient49 
3.2.4 Image processing and feature extraction 
The captured images were processed using CellProfiler11 (Broad Institute of MIT and Harvard). All 
cells found to be in contact with the border of an image were discarded, because the shape and 
structure of the cell would be interrupted by image border and thus potentially could provide 
misleading shape and texture information off the cells in contact with the border of the image. 
Features providing information about the cell were then extracted. From the DAPI channel the 
location of the cell within the individual image was extracted from the position of the cell nucleus. 
From the actin channel a total of 121 features were extracted, including 42 area shape features of 
which 30 was Zernike coefficients, 42 actin distribution measures, 14 actin intensity measures, 13 
==PAGE:36==
Method and Materials 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 34 of 102 
25.06.2015 
Haralick  texture features and 10 inter cell distance and relation measures. Finally from the tracker 
dye channel 14 tracker dye intensity measures were extracted to function as a positive control for 
classification of endothelial cells. In total, 135 distinct features of each cell were extracted plus 
some additional metadata such as on which sample, scan, and image the cell was identified. 
3.2.5 Data Processing 
The imaging data was initially analysed in CellProfiler analyst. Here, the data from a cell is paired 
with the images of the cells which provides a good overview for initial analysis and visual 
inspection of the segmented cells. All remaining data analysis and figure construction was 
performed using RStudio Version 0.99.486. R-code used in this thesis can be seen in Appendix D. 
From CellProfiler Analyst a naïve Bayes classifier was built to separate the cells in endothelial cells 
and fibroblasts based on the tracker dye intensity features. This initial classification provides the 
basis for subsequent classification error estimation of cells as well as class separation when cell 
specific features are inspected. The goal of a co-culture classifier is to be able to separate multiple 
cell types from each other independent of the underlying substrate. Therefore, the classifier must 
not be trained with features specific to the substrates. To ensure this, any features that could provide 
locational or sample specific information was removed, i.e. sample number, scan number, imaging 
date, and position. In addition, tracker dye features were removed. This left 121 features for each 
cell. 
Random forest classifiers were fitted to the data. The randomForest() function from the 
randomForest R-package68 was used to separate the two cell types, fibroblasts and endothelia cells. 
The number of trees grown in the forest and the size of the subset of features available for each tree 
are two basic parameters in the random forest algorithm that can be tweaked. Classification was 
done within a 10-fold cross validation where the data set is randomly divided into 10 equal parts. 
This was done to determine the optimal number of features available to each tree. In each cross-
validation fold nine parts are used to train the different classifiers and the remaining part is used to 
test the accuracy of the trained classifiers. Thereby, the error is tested on a part of the data that was 
not used for training and thus become an unbiased estimator of classification error. Within each 
cross-validation fold seven random forests were grown having either 1, 11 (default value), 25, 50, 
75, 100 or 121 randomly selected features available to each tree. In each forest 1000 trees were 
grown. The classifier training error was compared among the two cell types using a two-sided t-test. 
Because the ratio of fibroblast to endothelial cells was roughly 2:1 it would be assumed that a fitted 
classifier would favour correctly classifying the fibroblasts. In order to avoid this training bias the 
random forest algorithm was modified to randomly sample 3430 samples with replacement from 
==PAGE:37==
Method and Materials 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 35 of 102 
25.06.2015 
each of the two populations. Thereby each of the seven forests built within each cross-validation 
fold was trained using a population with equal representation from both populations. 
The output of the random forest algorithm provides training error estimates after each grown tree 
within a forest. Therefore, the output from the trained forests can also be used to determine the 
optimal number of trees to grow in a forest. 
3.2.6 Morphological changes along gradient 
One of the purposes of the study was to monitor changes along the gradient. Therefore the local 
positioning data of each cell within the individual images were converted to a global scale that 
describes cell placement on the sample as a whole. This was done by using the individual position 
of the cells within an image in combination with the image position relative to the other images 
within a scan and assigning a pillar height to each cell. The nano-pillar gradient is not completely 
linear due to stretching of the pillars as the substrate is extracted from the tool after injection 
moulding as visualized in Figure 3.4. However, it was decided that the small deviation from a 
completely linear gradient was negligible and consequently pillar height was assigned to cell 
position as a rectilinear increase from 0-250 nm across the 10 centre images of each scan.  
The data was prepared to investigate changes of specific features along the gradient. There were 
found large cell-to-cell variations in feature values along the gradient. Therefore, it was difficult to 
interpret the effects of the gradient on single cell measurements and it was decided to combine cells 
in a number of bins by position. The code for the function collectInBins() that accomplished this 
can be seen in Appendix D. The function starts by creating n*b position bins where n is the number 
of sections the user wants to divide each image into and b is the number of images in a scan. A 
position bin is then allocated to each cell. Within each bin the mean, median, the standard deviation 
of each feature value and the number of cells was calculated for each of the two cell types 
individually. It was later found that a function SummarySE()69 existed, this function can use the 
position bins generated by the collectInBins() function to calculate summary statistics mean, 
standard deviation and Confidence interval for each bin. It was modified to also produce median, 1th 
quantile and 4th quantile and has subsequently been used to generate several of the plots seen in the 
results section. The code for the SummarySE() function can also be seen in Appendix D. 
3.3 Monocyte cultures 
The original primary objective of working with the monocyte cultures was to build a classifier 
capable of separating monocyte M1 and M2 phenotypes. To investigate morphological differences 
of the M1 and M2 phenotype monocytes were seeded on flat topography substrates. Cytokines 
==PAGE:38==
Method and Materials 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 36 of 102 
25.06.2015 
inducing the monocytes to change to the M1 phenotype were added to half of the samples and 
cytokines inducing the monocytes to change to the M2 phenotype was added to the other half. In 
addition, samples of naïve monocytes on flat topography were produced as controls.  
The original secondary objective was to monitor the effects of nano-pillar, nano-pit and dual 
gradient topographies on naïve monocytes. Therefore, cells were seeded on the different types of 
topographies explained earlier. In addition to monitoring and describing the morphological changes 
of the naïve cells it was hoped that the M1 and M2 classifier built in the first step could detect if the 
topographies caused the cells to sway towards one of the two phenotypes at distinct points along the 
gradients. To work as positive control the samples were stained for calprotectin and mannose 
receptors that function as M1 and M2 markers respectively.  
The monocyte samples were prepared by a collaborator. The samples were imaged at the University 
of Glasgow by the author. In the following paragraphs the main points in the seeding and staining 
of monocytes will be elaborated. The complete seeding and staining procedures can be found in 
Appendix B 
3.3.1 Sample Seeding 
Cells were human CD14 positive human monocytes isolated from buffy coat of peripheral blood 
using CD14 microbeads. The buffy coat is the fraction of a blood sample that contains most of the 
white blood cells and platelets following centrifugation, Figure 3.6. 
 
Figure 3.6: Blood components after centrifugation70 
Using the Uniform Cell Seeding Device49, the cells were seeded onto nano-pillar, nano-pit, dual 
gradient substrates, and flat controls. Cells were seeded using a solution with concentration of 
6*103 cells per µm of growth medium and was incubated for a total of 6 days at 37C in 5% CO2. 
Half of the flat topography samples where seeded with IFN-gamma and GM-CSF pro-inflammatory 
cytokines to induce M1 phenotypes. The other half was seeded with IL-4 and M-CSF anti-
inflammatory cytokines to induce M2 phenotype. 
==PAGE:39==
Method and Materials 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 37 of 102 
25.06.2015 
3.3.2 Immunofluorescence Staining 
Following the incubation process the samples were stained. The DNA and actin of the cells should 
have been stained to provide a basis for segmentation and proper feature extraction but receptors 
that were distinct for the M1 and M2 phenotype should also have been stained. These receptor 
stains should provide positive control of cell phenotype in the later implemented monocyte 
phenotype classification algorithm. However, due to what must be described as miscommunication 
between the two involved research groups the first two datasets did not have actin staining on all 
samples. Instead samples that were stained for actin had no stain for phenotype and samples that 
were stained for phenotype had no actin stain. This was corrected on the final three samples sets 
therefrom and onwards all samples were stained for DNA and actin to ensure best possible basis for 
segmentation and in addition most other samples were stained for either M1 or M2 markers. A 
complete list of the individual samples their topography and their stains can be found in Appendix 
C. 
Following the incubation period cells were fixed in 4 % paraformaldehyde, and were permeabilized 
with 0.2% triton-X and thereafter unspecifically blocked by 5% normal goat serum to reduce 
chance of unspecific binding of stains. Actin was stained using Alexa Fluor® 488 Phalloidin 
(Invitrogen) and DNA was stained using DAPI. Phenotypic distinct markers where stained using 
two step immunoflourescent staining. The fluorescent markers used to stain actin, mannose and 
calprotectin differed between the sample sets due to changes in the staining process. These changes 
were necessary due to findings in imaging of the first two sample sets. A complete list of individual 
sample staining can be inspected in Appendix C. Following staining samples were embedded in 
mounting media (Floromount - Sigma), cover slips were put over the cells and sample were sealed 
using Unibond epoxy glue or clear nail polish. 
3.3.3 Microscopy 
Images of the monocyte samples were acquired using an Invitrogen EVOS FL Auto Cell Imaging 
System (Thermo-Fischer, USA) equipped with a 40x objective. The EVOS microscope has a 
motorized stage and provides light for the individual fluorescence channels through interchangeable 
light cubes. Images were captured with the built in monochrome camera and 3 distinct channels 
were automatically captured, for each of the fluorescent channels. The light cubes and the 
fluorescent channel captured are summarized in table 3.2. On flat samples images were captured at 
random locations on the sample. However, it was ensured that the images on the individual samples 
were equally spatially distributed. Low homogeneity of samples was discovered early in the process 
therefore very few systematic linear scans along the gradient were taken of topographical samples. 
Instead images were captured at random at equally spatial distributed locations of the samples.  
==PAGE:40==
Method and Materials 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 38 of 102 
25.06.2015 
When imaging samples for subsequent segmentation and feature extraction of which several 
features are based on intensity it is important to maintain constant light intensity, exposure, and 
gain. Otherwise the noise induced from changes in intensity might overshadow other observable 
differences in cell morphology. However, due to the relatively low bit depth (8-bit) the camera in 
the microscope and due to inhomogeneous fluorescence intensities from sample set to sample set 
and from sample to sample within sample sets it was not possible to keep light intensity, exposure, 
and gain constant for all samples. Low intensity stains demanded longer exposure and higher light 
intensity, while the low bit depth demanded lower intensity and gain to avoid clipping. To ensure 
comparability among similar samples it was tried to keep parameters constant within samples of 
same topography within a sample set.  
Table 3.2 Table of EVOS light cubes used for image capture and the corresponding fluorescent marker They captured 
Light Cube Fluorescent marker Excitation wave 
length (nm) 
Emission wave 
length (nm) 
DAPI 
DAPI 
357 
447 
GFP 
Alexa Flour 488 
470 
510 
Texas Red 
Rhodamine Red-X 
585 
624 
Cy5 
Cy5 
628 
692 
 
3.3.4 Image Processing and Data Analysis 
Initial attempts were made to process captured images using CellProfiler. However, due to the low 
homogeneity between samples attempts to segment images were not successful. The fifth and final 
sample set seemed promising on initial inspection. However, the data set was received late in the 
process. Therefore, the results of segmentation, feature extraction and subsequent data analysis 
could not be included within the time available for the completion of this thesis.  
==PAGE:41==
Results 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 39 of 102 
25.06.2015 
4. Results  
4.1  Automated label-free co-culture separation of endothelial cells and fibroblasts 
The overall test error was calculated to evaluate how well the random forest algorithm could 
classify the cells either as endothelia cells or fibroblasts. The test error was influenced by both the 
number of potential features in the feature subset, see Figure 4.1, and by the number of trees in each 
forest, see figure 4.2. It is seen in Figure 4.1 that for most forests the test error was around 17 %. 
Only the forest with one feature available for the generation of each tree significantly differs from 
the rest. The forest having all 121 features available for generation of trees is most similar to the 
forest with only 1 feature available and was found to be significant different from the others (p = 
0.026). While the median test error of this forest is still below 17% it seems that the quantiles are 
skewed towards higher error estimates for the forest where all 121 features were available. In 
Figure 4.1 it can be seen that within 100 trees the test error seems to have dropped to a level after 
which the test error seems to be more or less constant. 
 
Figure 4.1Boxplot of test error as a function of the number of features selected at random in the feature subset before the 
generation of each decision tree in the random forest. 1000 trees were generated for each forest.  
 
==PAGE:42==
Results 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 40 of 102 
25.06.2015 
 
Figure 4.2 Test error as a function of number in trees in each forest. 11 randomly selected features available to each tree in 
the forest. Solid blue line is the median test error. The dashed red lines are the 1st and 3rd quartiles of the test error. 
It was chosen to use 11 features in the generation of trees in the final random forest model. The 
classification was based on cells from the entire surface of the samples regardless of underlying 
topography. Here, the collective error rate for the classification was 16.72 %. It is seen in Table 4.1 
that endothelial cells have double error rate compared to fibroblasts.  
Table 4.1 Confusion matrix of the classification of endothelial cells and fibroblasts from the co-culture samples using the 
random forest classifier. The rows describe the true class and the columns describe the predicted class. 
 
Endothelial 
Fibroblast Error Rate 
Endothelial 
2917 
915 
0.239 
Fibroblast 
797 
5608 
0.124 
 
The random forest algorithm rank the features based on their importance in classification of the cell 
types. The importance is measured by two variables the mean decrease in accuracy and the mean 
decrease in Gini (node purity) when the feature is dropped from the algorithm. In Figure 4.3 it is 
seen that while many of the features appear on both lists there are inconsistencies in which features 
of the cells are considered the most important in separating the two cell types. The vast majority of 
important features are measures of texture. In the top five in both lists all but one are textural 
features. In the top five, three measures are the same in both lists. These are the Haralick feature 
sum of average actin, the variation of the actin intensity within the 9th of 10 contours, and the 
Haralick feature second angular moment. In Figure 4.4 can be seen the three features plotted against 
==PAGE:43==
Results 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 41 of 102 
25.06.2015 
each other. It can be seen in all of the plots that cells of similar type cluster together. However, in 
none of the plots the clusters of the two cell types are completely separated. It is seen that on 
average fibroblasts have higher intensity of actin in contour 9 of 10, higher average intensity of 
actin and lower second angular moment compared to endothelial cells. 
 
 
Figure 4.3 Plot of the importance of the first 20 features in classification of endothelial cells and fibroblasts selected by the 
random forest algorithm. MeanDecreaseGini is a measure of drop in node purity if feature is removed and 
MeanDecreasAccuracy is a measure of loss of accuracy if feature is removed. 
==PAGE:44==
Results 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 42 of 102 
25.06.2015 
 
Figure 4.4 Plot of the three most important features in separation of endothelial from fibroblasts Top lef is Fraction of actin 
in contour 9 of 10 plotted against the Haralick feature Sum Average of actin. Bottom row is the Haralick  feature second 
angular moment plotted against the Haralick  feature Sum of Average actin (left) and Fraction of actin in contour 9 of 10 
(right) 
In Figure 4.5 it is seen that the outlier score for the two cells types are similar, which is supported 
by that there was no significant difference was found between the two using non parametric 
wilcoxon test (p = 0.27). No single points are plotted outside the whiskers of the boxplot. This 
implies that no single observations differ more than 1.5 times the inter quartile range in any of the 
classes.
