Figure 4.5 Boxplot of outlier scores of the observations within each class. Based on the proximity measures from the 
randomForest algorithm 
In Figure 4.6 can be seen that best examples of fibroblasts (lowest outlier score) has branched 
shape, striated actin and clear nuclei. The worst examples of fibroblasts (highest outlier score) are 
out of focus overlapping cells presenting unclear actin structure and weak nuclei stain. Best 
examples of endothelial cells have clear nuclei, clean separation from background, and visible actin 
structures. Worst case endothelial cell examples are out of focus with unclear structure or 
overlapping with fibroblasts.

Figure 4.6 Examples of best and worst cases of the two cell types, fibroblasts and endothelia cells. Best are cells with lowest 
outlier score. Worst are cells with highest outlier score. Actin is red. Nuclei are blue. Endothelial cells seem more purple 
because the tracker dye stain layer (green colour) has been removed from the images. 
4.2 Nano-pillar gradient as a tool for co-culture analysis 
The montage of scans in Figure 4.7 shows the results of cell imaging. It is seen that endothelial cells 
(primarily green) are concentrated on the gradient while the majority of fibroblasts (primarily red) 
are placed outside the gradient. There appears to be a continuous drop in the number of fibroblasts 
as the pillar height increases and an abrupt increase in fibroblasts in the transition from the highest 
nano-pillars to flat. 
 
Figure 4.7 Montage of 4 scans of images along the gradient of a single sample. Horizontal Vertical black dashed lines mark 
the beginning and end of the gradient. Gradient of nano pillars increase from left to right. The green colour is the tracker dye 
marking endothelial cells. The red colour is the actin stain. The blue DAPI colouring of actin is not visible at this resolution. 
==PAGE:47==
Results 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 45 of 102 
25.06.2015 
The montage of scans in Figure 4.7 shows the results of cell imaging. It is seen that endothelial cells 
(primarily green) are concentrated on the gradient while the majority of fibroblasts (primarily red) 
are placed outside the gradient. There appears to be a continuous drop in the number of fibroblasts 
as the pillar height increases and an abrupt increase in fibroblasts in the transition from the highest 
nano-pillars to flat. 
 
Figure 4.8 Barplot of selected features from the cells presented in Figure 4.9. All values for the dataset were normalized with 
zero mean and standard deviation 1 before extracting the values for the selected cells. All the cells were from the same image 
(105) on the flat section outside of the gradient. 
In the barplot in Figure 4.8 can be seen differences in selected feature values measured for cells in 
the same image. The values of the entire dataset were normalized with zero mean and standard 
deviation 1 to allow them to be shown in one plot. Fluorescence microscopy images of the five cells 
from Figure 4.8 can be seen in Figure 4.9. It can be seen in Figure 4.8 that two endothelial cells 
found in vicinity of each other can vary greatly in perimeter, while having the same area. In 
addition, their values in the three texture features can be very different. In particular, the entropy is 
much lower for endothelial cell 1 and second angular moment is much higher. Fibroblast 2 and 3 
has a much higher part of their actin in in contour 9 of 10 compared to fibroblast 3 and much lower 
in contour 5 and 7 compared to fibroblast 3. Fibroblast 3 has high entropy compared to 1 and 2.

 (A) Endothelial cell

Figure 4.9 Endothelial cells and fibroblasts found on same image (image 105). The image was from a flat section outside the 
gradient. Showing variations among cells of same type. Red: actin. Green: nuclei. A and B endothelial cells. C, D and E are 
fibroblasts. 
Figure 4.9 shows different morphological characteristics for the two cell types. The cells were taken 
from the same image. In the top row is seen the endothelial cells. It is seen that (B) has a much very 
irregular perimeter while (A) is round. Actin is present but not clearly structured in either cell. 
Bottom row of Figure 4.9 are fibroblasts. (C) has clear unidirectional striations of actin fibres while 
(D) has actin along the edge. (E) has the clear morphology of a moving fibroblast with convex 
leading edge heavy in actin (top) and trailing protrusions (bottom).  
 
==PAGE:49==
Results 
S.S.Lillelund

25.06.2015 
 
Figure 4.10 shows the raw data of cell area as a function of nano-pillar gradient. Red: Endothelial cells. Blue: fibroblasts. The 
greyscale background: nano-pillar gradient. 
The raw area measurements are visualized along the sample the increasing grey gradient in the 
background indicate the sample nano-pillar gradient increasing from left to right, shown in Figure 
4.10. It is seen that area stretch from 0 to around10000 pixels among cells of equal type even on 
pillars of comparable height. There seems to be a slight decrease in variation of area of fibroblasts 
from around100 nm to around 250 nm. However, it is very hard to conclude something from this 
representation of the data. This order of variation in data at similar pillar heights were seen at all 
other features (not presented) 
 
Figure 4.11 Left: Cell area as a function of nano-pillar gradient constructed by moving average. Right: Frequency content of 
mean intensity data. Red solid are endothelial cells. Blue dashed is fibroblasts. The greyscale background represents the 
nano-pillar gradient.

Figure 4.11 left shows how the area of the cells changes across the gradient. The curve was 
constructed by taking the mean of the area of each cell type within a sliding window half the width 
of an image and with half window overlap in order to create equally spaced data point along the 
gradient axis and to smooth short term fluctuation while still retaining trends. This allowed 
construction of the frequency spectrum seen in Fejl! Henvisningskilden blev ikke fundet.Figure 
4.11 right using the fast Fourier transform. It can be seen that the area of endothelial cells increases 
in the interface between the flat surface and the beginning of the low pillars. However, after the 
spike at the interface the area seems constant across the gradient of pillars. On the other hand, the 
fibroblasts seem to have a constant area along the flat topographies on both sides of the gradient. 
However, along the gradient the area continuously drops in size until the reach a pillar height 
between 150 and 200 nm at which point the size increases again. In addition, it is noted that for both 
cell types the curves in the plot seems to have periodic fluctuations at a frequency of roughly 25 
nm-1 equivalent of the width of an image of the gradient. This is confirmed by the frequency content 
that shows that the period of the fluctuations is at frequency of 1 image-1. Focusing on one of the 
intervals representing an image (0-25 nm, 25-50 nm etc.) it is clear that within each image the area 
is low at the edges and increase towards the centre of and image.  
 
 
Figure 4.12 Left: Mean intensity of actin as a function of nano-pillar gradient constructed by moving average. Right: 
Frequency content of mean intensity data. Red solid lines: endothelial cells. Blue dashed lines: fibroblasts. Greyscale 
background: nano-pillar gradient. 
The mean intensity of actin is the average pixel intensity within the cell and is visualized for the 
dataset in Figure 4.12. It is seen that both endothelial cells and fibroblasts increase in mean intensity 
of the actin stain across the gradient until a peak height up to approximately 125 nm from here the

endothelial cells drop quickly in mean intensity while the fibroblast loses intensity slower. Periodic

increase around amplitude a 1 image-1 in Figure 4.12 right confirms the observation. Focusing on a

the intensity peaks around the centre. The other intensity measures (not shown) showed the exact 
same image to image periodic trends. 
4.3 The impact of the topographical gradient on endothelial and fibroblast co-culture 
behaviour 
Only selected features of the available 121 features for each cell are presented in this section. The 
remaining features either showed results similar to those presented or did not show results that 
would improve the understanding of the cell behaviour. It must be noted that great inhomogeneity 
was found among the cells of equal type along the gradient. Even among cells placed at comparable 
pillar heights. Therefore, care must be taken when conclusions are made based on the results. 
4.3.1 Distance to neighbouring cells 
Four measures related to how neighbouring cells interact are shown in Figure 4.13. It is seen that 
there are more fibroblasts than endothelial cells. The number of endothelial cells remains almost 
constant throughout the gradient, disregarding minor fluctuations. The number of fibroblasts 
decreases continuously from onset of the gradient until reaching a seemingly constant level of 
around 100 cells per bin at a pillar height of 150 nm. This number of fibroblasts is maintained until 
the end of the gradient where the number of fibroblast increases to the level before the gradient.  
Figure 4.13 top right shows the distance measured in pixels between pairs of cells closest to each 
other. It is seen that the distance between endothelial cells is largest in the interface between the flat 
topography and the gradient, and that the distance decreases across the gradient with a minimum 
around a pillar height of 125 nm. Hereafter, the distance increases at a similar rate to reach the same 
inter-cell distance after the gradient as before the gradient. The fibroblasts gets continuously closer 
until a minimum at a pillar height of 175 nm after which the distance between the fibroblast 
increases to a level equal to the distance before the gradient.  
Data visualized in Figure 4.13 bottom left represent a measure of how much the cells touch each 
other. On average the fibroblasts touch neighbouring cells more than endothelia cells. The levels of 
touching for the fibroblast are equal on both the flat surfaces outside the gradient. Within the 
gradient fibroblasts touch more and more cells until a peak at a pillar height of 175 nm before 
decreasing again. Endothelial cells touch more cells until a peak at a pillar height of100 nm 
==PAGE:52==
Results 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 50 of 102 
25.06.2015 
remaining at the same level until around a pillar height of 200 nm before decreasing. In Figure 4.12 
bottom right is seen the number of neighbours touching the cell. On average one cell touches 
endothelial cells while two cells touch fibroblast outside the gradient and three inside the gradient. 
 
 
Figure 4.13 Plots of features related to how cells relate to each other. All curves are median values calculated within sections 
half the width of an image. Top left: the number of cells in the bins that are the foundation for the median calculations. Top 
right: distance to closest neighbour. Bottom left: Percent of pixels in the perimeter of the cell that is in contact with a 
neighbouring cell. Bottom right: the number of cells touching a cell. Median taken over all recorded scans for sections with 
equal pillar height. Red solid lines: endothelial cells, blue dashed lines: fibroblast. Greyscale background: nano-pillar 
gradient of the underlying substrate. Vertical dashed lines: end of gradients observations. Before and after the gradient are 
the two flat surfaces.

4.3.2 Morphology 
In Figure 4.14 is seen how the morphology of the cells changes in response to the surface gradient. 
The arean(top left) and perimeter (top right) follow similar patterns. The area and perimeter of the 
fibroblasts seem to have constant values on the flat topography on both sides of the gradient. 
However, on the gradient the area and perimeter decreases until a pillar height of approximately 
150nm at which point it maintains an almost constant level until rapidly increasing to the flat 
surface level at the interface between the gradient and flat surface. The endothelial cells have a

gradient at a pillar height of approximately 0 nm. 
The orientation of the cells measured by the direction of the cell along the long axis relative to the 
image axis is shown in figure 4.14 bottom left. It is seen that the orientation fluctuates between ± 25 
degrees without any clear trend across the gradient except for a slight drop in orientation on the 
gradient for both cell types. 
The extend of the cells across the gradient is shown in Figure 4.14 bottom right and is a measure of 
the area of the cell divided by the area of the smallest ellipse that covers the entire cell. Thereby, the 
extend becomes a measure of cell regularity as a perfect circular cell would have an extend of 1 and 
irregular cells will have extend lower than 1. It is seen that endothelial cells seem to have equal 
extend on both sides of the gradient and a slight drop i across the gradient with minimum at pillars 
above approximately 150 nm in height. Fibroblasts have the opposite characteristics, as they have 
equal low levels of extend in the flat regions and an increase across the gradient peaking at a pillar 
height of approximately 150-200.

equal pillar height. Red solid are endothelial cells, blue dashed are fibroblasts. The greyscale gradient represents the nano-
pillar gradient of the underlying substrate. Vertical dashed lines shows end of gradients observations outside gradient are the 
two flat sections before and after the gradient on the substrate.

divided by the number of pixels in the cell thereby correcting for size. It is seen that for both cell 
types the actin intensity increases along the gradient until about hallway through where it starts

4.4 Challenges in processing monocyte samples for automated label-free phenotype 
separation 
Almost a thousand images were captured of the monocytes sample sets received from collaborator 
creating. Some of which are presented in the following paragraphs. 
 
Figure 4.18 Image of a sample set received from collaborator. 
 
 
 
Figure 4.19 Examples of cell from the first two samples set that were not actin stained. Left to right: All channels, mannose 
receptor marker channel, Calprotectin marker channel and nucleus channel. Note that the Calprotectin channel was 
captured with higher exposure time, gain and light intensity compared to the calprotectin channels of the M1 sample. A: M1 
sample, B: M2 sample. 
The samples depicted in Figure 4.18 are without a doubt the worst set received from the 
collaborator. In the samples in the top row marked 5 to 8 it is clear that the coverslip is not in 
complete contact with the substrates as liquid are still caught between the glass slide and the 
==PAGE:58==
Results 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 56 of 102 
25.06.2015 
substrate. In the bottom row the glass slide fits better on the samples however pieces of tissue paper 
are attached to the nail polish used to seal the samples. In the top left sample marked 8 the coverslip 
is larger than the substrate surface.  
In Figure 4.19 can be seen examples of captured fluorescent channels for samples from the first two 
datasets that were not stained for actin. It is seen that the M1 samples have high intensity 
calprotectin stain and high intensity DAPI while maintaining low mannose intensity. In the M2 
samples it is seen that there is high mannose intensity and nucleus intensity. The calprotectin also 
appears high but in the M2 samples of the first two data sets the calprotectin channel was set to 
higher gain, exposure time, and light intensity in order to make it stand out from the background. 
Therefore, the intensity of the stain in this channel was much lower in reality. 
 
 
Figure 4.20 Examples of issues with cell homogeneity on monocyte samples. A: a cluster of cell. B: diffused light around cells 
and C: low intensity stain and cell fragments 
The images captured of the monocytes showed many problems with heterogeneity within the 
samples. This led to improper feature extraction and thus the samples could not be used. Three 
examples of issues with the cell heterogeneity Figure 4.20. The first issue was clustering of cells 
shown in Figure 4.19.A. Here it can be seen that the nuclei are well separated but the actin and 
mannose stain are a one big cluster with few individual cells visible why no features can be 
extracted on cell level. A second issue, shown in Figure 4.20.B, is caused by liquid bobbles under 
the coverslip. The bobbles act as lenses and diffracts the light making the actin stain unclear. A 
third issue was that many cells had very weak actin stain and nucleus stain, as shown in figure 
4.20.C. In addition, several fragments light up with high intensity. 
 
==PAGE:59==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 57 of 102 
25.06.2015 
5. Discussion 
5.1 Automated label-free co-culture separation of endothelial cells and fibroblasts 
The random forest algorithm was used to classify the cells as either fibroblasts or endothelia cells 
based on features extracted from imaging of the cells. In the random forest algorithm values for 
different settings has to be chosen. Within 10-fold cross-validation the prediction error of forests 
having different number of features available to build each tree were estimated. Random forests 
built with only 1 randomly selected feature for each tree is insufficient to fully utilize the 
functionality of the algorithm (Figure 4.1) at least if the forest only contains 1000 trees. The default 
setting in the randomForest R algorithm68 is the square root of the total number of features 
available, which in this case this was 11 features. When building the random forest with use of the 
default value fewer trees was necessary (Figure 4.2) to provide higher accuracy compared to when 
only one feature was available. This makes sense, as having more features available in each forest 
allows each forest to create decision boundaries along multiple dimensions of the feature set. 
Having all 121 features available to build each tree of the forest it is a special case of the random 
forest equivalent of bootstrapping samples to produce an ensample of unpruned decision trees. 
While bootstrapping makes the trees less susceptible to misclassification due to fluctuations in the 
training set66 the trees in the forest might not be sufficiently uncorrelated to utilize the advantage of 
the random forest because they are all based on the same features. Unpruned decision trees have a 
tendency to overfit67. Bootstrapping and ensambling can usually alleviate some of these 
problems64,65 but when all the trees are based on the same features the trees correlate. Therefore, the 
forest will converge towards the same overfitting effect as would be seen in a normal decision tree. 
This is probably why the error distribution of the forest having all 121 features available for each 
tree is skewed toward higher error (Figure 4.1) compared to forest built with fewer trees available. 
It is possible that more trees would enhance this effect. Using the default values for the number of 
features randomly chosen to build the forest give consistently good classification results64,65 and is 
clearly found to be sufficient to produce continuous low levels of error that are comparable to the 
trees using more features in each tree. Therefore it was chosen to use the default value in the final 
analyses. 
It seems that 100 trees should be sufficient to get consistently error rates below 17%. A forest of 
100 trees takes roughly 96 seconds to generate while a forest of a 1000 takes roughly 960 seconds. 
Thereby if 100 trees are sufficient to produce reliable classification of cells there will be no need to 
==PAGE:60==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 58 of 102 
25.06.2015 
waste time on building extra trees which will be an advantage when working with large data sets. 
This is consistent with previous findings64. 
The increased misclassification of endothelial cells compared to fibroblasts can be because 
classifiers favour the largest group in a data set71. In this study care was taken to ensure that the 
bootstrapped datasets were sampled equally from both classes. This should reduce the accuracy bias 
of the predictor training towards the overrepresented class. The classifier was also trained without 
adjusting for class size. This reduced the accuracy and made misclassification of endothelial raise to 
40% and fibroblast misclassification drop to 2-4%, data not shown. In extremely unbalanced 
situations such as this (2:1) bagging has been found insufficient in rendering proper predictions for 
the minority class71 therefore it could be considered to employ alternatives in future studies. The 
variation among the cells, seen in Figure 4.6 and 4.9, is a testament to how different the cell can be 
that the algorithm has to overcome. 
An error rate of 16.72 % is equivalent to the error rate found by Reynolds49 for co-cultures of 
fibroblasts and endothelial cells using a naïve Bayes classifier. No increase in accuracy was gained 
using the random forest method. However, the method has the advantage of providing easy 
interpretable measures of importance of features and measure of class outliers. Classification of 
cells from images by experts usually relies on cell specific markers and or histologic knowledge of 
the tissue from which the cells originate. In some cases, expert evaluation is still superior to 
automated classification11. This is mainly because computers rely on quantifying all necessary 
information and use mathematical models to predict classes while the human eye can see patterns 
and recall information about the cell images that might lead to a more abstract set of decisions 
leading to the final classification. However, Reynolds 49 showed that letting experts separate 
endothelial cells from fibroblasts based solely on images stained for DNA and actin showed no 
better results than flipping a coin. The precision of the data set in the present study is based upon 
the initial classification of the cells using the intensity of tracker dye, which has been shown to 
produce classification of endothelial fibroblast cultures at accuracy above 98% 49. Thereby while 
the random forest algorithm show an advantage in speed of analysis and accuracy compared to 
visual inspection there is still room for improvement in the label-free classification of endothelial 
cells and fibroblasts.  
The importance of features in classification is a measure of decrease in accuracy and decrease in 
node purity. They have both merits. The mean decrease accuracy is based on the out-of-bag 
classification error. Thereby, it can be considered as a more unbiased estimator than the node purity 
gained. The latter is based on local drop in impurity in the tree nodes during training. In general it 
seems that the different actin radial distribution measures are important in the classification of cells 
==PAGE:61==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 59 of 102 
25.06.2015 
as either fibroblast or endothelia cells (Figure 4.3). However, the features consistently present in the 
top 5 for both measures are the two Haralick textural features called the sum of average actin and 
second angular moment together with the fraction of intensity of actin stain present in the 9th of 10 
contours which is the second closest to the cell border. Actin structure as important separators 
makes sense. Fibroblast are characterised with striated stress fibres when spread out27 and high 
density leading edge when mobile27,29. Endothelia cells, on the other hand, have a polygonal 
morphology and actin bundled along the perimeter in stationary cells. However, endothelial cells 
can also align shape and actin in directions perpendicular to stress25 which could make them seem 
more like fibroblasts. Form factor, a measure of roundness is also found to be important in 
separation of the two cell classes (Figure 4.3) Which makes sense as fibroblasts are usually reported 
to be oblong with protrusions27 compared to the more circular endothelia cells25,72. Many other 
characteristic differences might usually be seen between the two cell types. However, both cell 
types are very susceptible to changes in their underlying substrate27,72 therefore the gradient of the 
substrate might confound some of the typical difference among the two cell types. 
Just using one of these features does not clearly separate in two cell types (Figure 4.4) even though 
the random forest can separate them with a low error. Therefore, it must be the combined effort of 
decisions across all the available features that provides the accuracy in classification. The radial 
distribution features predominantly separate the two classes. These are measures of how the actin 
intensity is distributed among a specified number of concentric contours within the cell. Both the 
differences in mean fraction of actin within contours and the distribution of actin in slices 
tangentially to the contours are important.  
It is interesting to note that no distinct outliers seem to be present in the data set based on the 1.5* 
inter quartile range as specified by McGill73 and outlier scores are similar for the two cell types 
(Figure 4.5). However, the fibroblasts seem to have observations further outside the quartile range 
compared to endothelial cells. Cells that are hard to classify (Figure 4.6 C & D) were seen to be 
cells that were out of focus or overlapped highly with other cells. The consistent segmentation is 
crucial to correct feature extraction11. Overlapping cells hinders proper and while overlapping cells 
cannot be fully avoided in living cells, it might be possible in future studies to weed out highly 
overlapping cells. However, many techniques exist dealing with out of focus microscopy images. 
As an example Selinummi et al.74 has shown that using images of the same area focused in different 
planes it was possible to segments cell even without the use of fluorescent stains. 
==PAGE:62==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 60 of 102 
25.06.2015 
5.2 Nano-pillar gradient as a tool for co-culture analysis 
High-throughput image processing in conjunction with nano-structure gradients to investigate the 
continuous changes in morphology of cells can impose some analytical challenges. These include in 
particular issues with high variance within images and converting a discrete set of images along a 
scan line that are representative of the continuous changes along a gradient. 
The work presented here relies on co-cultures. Co-cultures are cultures of two or more cell types or 
cell phenotypes. This can provide a source of variance. Cells interact with each other which can 
change their morphology and behaviour39. When endothelial cells interact under the right 
circumstances they form a monolayer of tight junctions as they, in their natural state, construct the 
cell lining of the cardiovascular and lymphatic systems75. Fibroblasts on the other hand interact with 
the ECM to fulfil their function of laying out new ECM fibres5 and are only found to contact 
neighbouring cells through protrusions with limited contact surface27. In addition, they are not 
limited to monolayers and move over and around other cells. . However it is unclear how the co-
interaction of fibroblast and endothelial co-cultures will be reflected in morphology.  
Attempts to use statistical measure to determine significant differences between sections along the 
gradient, not shown, proved difficult as there were even found statistical significant differences (p < 
0.05) between cells of same type in populations of neighbouring bins on the flat part of the 
substrate. The variation among the cells of equal cell type taken from the same image on the flat 
part of the substrate (Figure 4.8 and 4.9) are indicative of the great variations among cell that exist 
on the substrate and possibly why it is difficult to determine significant changes along the gradient. 
The gradient might show in some places to reduce the variation between cells as they are forced to 
conform to their environment76 which is also consistent with the decrease in cell variance found by 
Muhammad et al.77 Large variations in perimeter and texture were seen.  
Fibroblast can react to structural changes down to the nm scale19. However, it is not clear if these 
complicated changes can be monitored at the level of detail provided by a 10x image. In this study 
was seen a slight decrease in variance in area (Figure 4.10) on pillar height ranging from 100 nm to 
250 nm However, this could easily be due to the drop in number of cells at these pillar heights 
(Figure 4.13) more than due to homogenous nature of cells due to pillar height. Pillars outside this 
interval might be too small in height to push a high enough proportion of the cells towards reacting 
to the surface topography thereby increasing the variance of cell morphology in these locations.  
The algorithm that segments the images used by CellProfiler removes cells that have pixels at the 
edge of the image. This is done to avoid clipping the cells and extracting false information about the 
edge cells. The repeating pattern of increased area at centres of images and lowered at the edges 
==PAGE:63==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 61 of 102 
25.06.2015 
indicates that there exist a higher probability of large cells touching the edge of the image (Figure 
4.11). These cells are therefore being cut which causes the majority of cells at the image edge to be 
interpreted as small cells. This can skew the data towards smaller cells compared to the actual 
population thus not providing a representative measure of the population. Cells decrease in area at 
loss of contact with a surface25. Thereby, it might result in an overrepresentation of cells that are not 
adequately attached to the surface and therefore not properly reflects the effect of the surface. In 
addition, the limited resolution of the camera and the spread of emitted light will cause smaller actin 
structures to be less clearly imaged than larger structures. Therefore, it will be more difficult to 
extract meaningful texture information from small cells compared to large cells. 
When fluorescence microscopy images are taken the light hitting the detector is never completely 
homogenous11. The light hitting the detector will usually be stronger in the centre and weaker at the 
edges of the image. The intensity data confirms this as the mean intensity peaks at the centre of the 
image and drops at the edges (Figure 4.12). This trend is confounded with the cell area which could 
skew data towards lower intensity in smaller cells. However the mean intensity is corrected for cell 
size and thus should reduce the confounding effect of cell size. Homogenous illumination is 
essential for reliable segmentation of cells11 because it relies on the intensity of the image 
background relative to the intensity of the cell staining. In addition, the results of this study indicate 
that the heterogeneous illumination influence intensity features and in extension potentially textural 
features that are based on intensity measurements. In addition, the heterogeneous illumination 
might also have increased the variance in other measurements due to inhomogeneous segmentation.  
In order for the high throughput imaging and gradient substrate to be effective as a tool it is clear 
that changes must be made to the recording and segmentation procedure. It is clear from the 
extracted features that illumination correction on this dataset is warranted. Many excellent 
illumination correction algorithms exist and could without a doubt be successfully applied to this 
data set reducing a source of noise. To avoid cutting cells at the edge of cells scans of imaging 
could be performed with a third of an image width overlap and with two extra scan lines with a 
third of an image height overlap one on each side of the original scan line. Thereby, it would be 
possible to catch the entire perimeter of the edge cells of the original scan line and the edge cells 
could successfully be segmented. This would definitely cause some cells to be classified more than 
once, but these replicates could easily be removed in post processing. 
==PAGE:64==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 62 of 102 
25.06.2015 
5.3 The impact of the topographical gradient on endothelial and fibroblast co-culture 
behaviour 
Fibroblasts and endothelial cells were seeded with equal concentrations before incubation. The 
number of fibroblast was found to higher than for endothelial cells after incubation. This is due to 
the very high proliferation rate of fibroblasts27. The number of fibroblasts decreased along the entire 
gradient, whereas the gradient did not seem to influence the number of endothelial cells. 
The area of the cells dropped across the gradient. This is supported by the findings of Dalby et al.31 
who found decrease in fibroblast area at a pillar height of 160 nm and Andersson et al78 and 
Muhammad et al77 who found that endothelial cells grown on pillars 100 nm and 250 nm in height 
respectively were significantly smaller compared to flat surface. Spreading of cells has before been 
used as a measure of contact adhesion as well attached cells spread more79. Poorly spread cells will 
have smaller area than well spread cells. The drop in fibroblast area together with the drop in cell 
number and the change in cell extend seen at pillars height of 150 nm to 250 nm seems to indicated 
that fibroblast at this pillar height actively seeks to remove themselves from the pillars. Some 
surfaces have shown to decrease proliferation rate in fibroblasts80. Therefore the drop in the number 
of cells could also be from failure to proliferate. proliferation demands contact adhesion21 and 
poorly spread cells are often a sign of weak attachment79. Thereby, the drop in area could be 
indicative of poorly spread cells. Therefore, the high pillars could lead to weak attachment of cells 
leading to poor spread and diminished proliferation. Weakly attached cells will seek out better 
attachment through protrusion retraction cycles40 or eventually perish8 if contact is lost completely. 
However, the drop in area could be confounded with the decrease in the number of cells and the 
effect the segmentation algorithm used has on cell size. If there are fewer cells in an image and the 
segmentation algorithm favours keeping smaller cells a drop in cell count would probably result in a 
drop in cell area.  
It might be wanted to create a biomaterial that reduce the number of fibroblasts migrating onto the 
surface or fibroblast proliferating on the surface while still stimulating a migration of endothelia 
cells onto the surface. This could be the case for stents18. If this is the case, then biomaterials with 
pillar heights higher than 150 nm in height are worthy of further investigation.  
Endothelial cells adhere to other endothelial cells to form monolayers19,25, while fibroblasts has a 
limited contact to each other through long protrusions in their natural environment27. Therefore, it is 
particular interesting that fibroblast was in closer contact with neighbouring cells than endothelial 
cells in this study. The number of fibroblasts dropped but the remaining cells stuck closer together. 
This might indicate that the culture change from a more uniform distribution in the flat surface to a 
clustering of cells along the gradient. The effects have not been monitored in real time. Therefore, it 
==PAGE:65==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 63 of 102 
25.06.2015 
is difficult to explain the underlying reason for the clustering of cells along the gradient. However, 
cell-to-cell contact can influence cell behaviour, orientation, and movement patterns1,5,39. Therefore, 
it might be interesting to investigate how this cell clustering is initiated in a future study. 
The area and the perimeter of the both cells types follow similar patterns across the gradient (Figure 
4.14). This is indicative of the cells retaining their overall shape across the gradient as two cells 
with the same perimeter would have a smaller area if the cell was elliptical compared to circular. 
The drop in perimeter is 5 % less than that of the area indicative of a change in size. However, this 
is within the range of error. Therefore, it would not be prudent to conclude that the shape changes 
based on this information. However, the irregularity of the cell changed across the gradient, shown 
by the extend. This might be indicative of changes in cell morphology as a response to the gradient, 
and that fibroblast become slightly less irregular on higher pillar heights compared to flat surface 
and lower pillar heights. In the same interval it seems endothelial cells becomes more irregular. 
Cells move to adapt. They move across surfaces through focal adhesion1. Cells sense their 
environment through protrusion and retraction of the cell membrane. Cells that actively engage in 
movement and have a good foothold will have a more regular border compared to the ruffled border 
that can come from weak adhesion40. The changes in orientation of the cells seem minimal if at all 
present indicating that the cells probably not align along the major axis in accordance with the 
substrate.  
The actin cytoskeleton is the internal structural frame of the cell. It regulates function as well as 
shape1,38,40,76. A cell adapting to topography change the spatial organization of the actin 
cytoskeleton38 as the link between the contact with the topography and the resulting cellular 
response is dependent on actin polymerization1. Focal adhesions are initiated in the periphery of the 
cell1. Therefore, it would be assumed that the actin intensity of the edges and the cell as a whole 
would increase if the cell actively adapts. While it is hard to see a change in the distribution of actin 
within the cells across the gradient (Figure 4.16) it is clear that endothelial cells have the actin 
closer to the nucleus compared to the fibroblasts. The change in radial variation of contours (Figure 
4.17) indicates that variation in actin intensity along a direction tangential to a contour increase for 
both fibroblasts and endothelial cells when moving towards the centre of the gradient. This 
indicates that the actin structure is not as equally distributed as on the flat surface. However, the 
corresponding drop in entropy for the endothelial cells (Figure 4.17) might at first glance seem to 
contradict this. A lower entropy would indicate a more homogenous distribution of actin. If the 
actin cytoskeleton is well structured with pronounced bundles of fibres stretching from the nucleus 
to the edge of the cell separated by areas of the cell without pronounced actin structure it could have 
a low entropy while still maintaining high variation in actin intensity tangentially to a contour. This 
==PAGE:66==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 64 of 102 
25.06.2015 
kind of structure would be characteristic of a cell that is actively adapting to its extracellular 
environment by building strong cytoskeletal structures. The constant entropy of the fibroblasts 
along the gradient (Figure 4.17) compared to the increasing variation in intensity tangentially to a 
contour (Figure 4.17) is indicative of a cell that might have redistributed the actin to uneven 
locations within the cell but has not build strong homogenous patterns of actin structures. This is 
consistent with the weak stress fibres in fibroblasts observed by Dalby et al.81 on nano-pillars 100 
nm tall. 
There is little doubt that the nano-pillars influence fibroblast morphology and that the gradient 
continuously affects the cells. For the majority of the features chosen to show results from, it can be 
seen that fibroblasts react almost linearly up to a plateau that starts at around pillar heights of 100 
nm and end around a pillar height of 200 nm. It appears that the fibroblasts actively try to remove 
themselves from the high pillars or fail to proliferate. This is seen by the decrease in cell number as 
the pillar height increases (Figure 4.13) and the extreme increase in number of cells when the 
switch from the highest pillars to the flat surface occurs. This behaviour is underlined by the change 
in shape, the increase in actin intensity (Figure 4.15) and the reconfiguration of the actin structure 
(Figure 4.17) that occurs in the same interval of pillar heights. The drop in area of the fibroblasts in 
the same interval also attests to the indications that the fibroblast really does not want to stay on the 
tall pillars and the ones that are still present seems to have reduced adhesion as seen by the decrease 
in area and the increase in extend. However this observation an also be due to the segmentation 
algorithm 
Endothelial cells seem to be less affected by the topography. The cell number remains mostly 
constant across the surface and area seems unaffected. However, the increased intensity of actin and 
the drop in extend could be indicative that the cells adapt to the pillars by changing their 
morphology. This is reinforced by the drop in entropy and increase in variation in actin. This 
indicates that the endothelial cells on the flat surface have less well defined and well-structured 
actin compared to the endothelial cells on the nano-pillars.  
By looking for peaks and changes in feature values in the topographical landscape it will be 
possible to create non-gradient samples with discrete pillar height at point that have shown 
interesting trends along the gradient and use these discrete samples through biochemical and image 
analysis to perform functional and morphological investigation respectively. Repeating experiments 
on discrete samples with topographies based on topography heights of interest found using the 
gradients could potentially provide more homogeneous results compared to the noisy results of the 
gradient thus providing a better statistical basis to draw conclusions about the effect of the 
topography. The topographical gradients together with the current segmentation and analysis 
==PAGE:67==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 65 of 102 
25.06.2015 
method can be used as an investigative tool to provide a foundation upon which the investigator can 
expand their investigations to discrete samples with single size topographical features. 
5.4 Challenges in processing monocyte samples for automated label-free phenotype 
separation 
The original focus of the work of this thesis was to investigate samples of human monocytes in 
order to establish if it was possible to use automated imaging and feature extraction to separate M1 
from M2 phenotypes of monocytes. In addition, it was wanted to use automated imaging of 
different gradients of nano-features to examine how the gradients influence the monocytes and if 
possible to find locations along any of the gradients that could be considered to induce changes in 
morphology in the naive monocytes comparable to one of the two phenotypes. However, it was 
found that the samples received were not suitable for automated imaging and analysis. At least one 
useable flat sample of M1 monocytes and one usable flat sample of M2 monocytes were needed in 
order to investigate if measurable morphological differences existed within the two populations. 
However, in the first two sample sets the actin of the cells was not coloured. This was because the 
laboratory creating the samples used the same fluorescent marker to colour actin and mannose 
receptors. To avoid interference, the laboratory decided not to colour the actin in the samples 
coloured for mannose receptors. Our entire segmentation was based on the actin stain and for 
accurate segmentation and subsequent comparison it is important to have an equal reference point11. 
This could of course not be established without an actin stain. It took a while for the author to 
realise that the actin stain was missing due to large differences in stain intensity between samples. 
Two months passed from preparation of the first sample set was completed until it was imaged by 
the author. This is not advisable as the fluorescent markers can bleach50. This leads to less detail in 
the stain and decrease in and heterogeneity of intensity of stains. It was thought that this was the 
reason for the lack of usable actin stain in the first sample set. In the remaining four sample sets 
imaging was commenced within a week of completion. 
In the next three sample sets a new fluorescent marker emitting light in another spectrum than the 
actin stain was purchased and used by the University of Nottingham that would allow for the 
staining of actin and mannose within the same samples. However, it was found that the two sample 
sets had very heterogeneous populations characterised by large areas almost free of cells, areas with 
cells that reminded more of cell fragments and finally big clusters of overlapping cells. The fifth 
sample set showed promising results however there were no more time in the project to complete 
analyses of the set. 
==PAGE:68==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 66 of 102 
25.06.2015 
In total five sample sets of monocytes seeded on topography were produced and almost a thousand 
images were taken at random locations across the samples. Before automated scanning of the 
samples it was wanted to perform manual imaging to inspect if the samples were suitable for 
automated imaging and consequent analysis. All the 1000 images were taken manually with manual 
focusing due to the subpar autofocusing algorithm of the EVOS microscope. This was very time 
consuming but necessary to screen the quality of the samples. Unfortunately, it was found that no 
samples sets were considered homogenous enough to perform high throughput analysis50 
Few results could however be extracted from the monocytes imaging. Mannose had a clearly higher 
intensity on the M2 samples compared to M1 indicating that the mannose stain is an efficient 
marker of the M2 phenotype (Figure 4.19). The calprotectin intensity was higher in the M1 samples 
showing this as an effective marker of M1 cells. However, this is not clear from Figure 4.19. The 
gain and exposure of the calprotectin channels was increased on the M2 samples. This was because 
it was thought to be actin staining at the time of imaging and a clear image of a comparable cell 
wide stain such as actin, was needed for segmentation11. However, varying the exposure and gain 
from sample to sample will not be a valid approach to automated imaging as it will make the 
extracted features incomparable. But for the initial screening it was necessary in order to locate the 
cells. The studies done on the final three datasets were done with comparable exposure, gain and 
light intensity.  
Many different configurations of unusable images were captured (Figure 4.20). Clusters makes it 
impossible to segment the cells into well-defined cells whose segmentation is not compromised by 
the overlapping cells and thereby provides unreliable shape, area, and textural measurements. In 
addition, the intensity of the stains becomes so great in clusters that the intensity in large parts of 
the cluster is clipped by the lack of sufficient bit-depth of the camera. Thereby, even if it was 
possible to segment the measurements, they would still be unreliable. While the diffused light 
caused by bobbles might not interrupt segmentation they will erase any structure in the actin 
skeleton thus rendering textural measurements invalid.   
Lichtman et al.50 list several reasons why fluorescence microscopy might fail including bleaching, 
low detector sensitivity, and properties of light path (like opaque surfaces or bobbles) which were 
violated on many of the samples. While some sample sets seemed rushed in their preparation with 
bobbles, clumps of nail polish and attached tissue paper others looked fine at first glance. However, 
the microscopy then showed that either stain intensity or cell culture heterogeneity made the 
samples unusable. The fifth and final data set showed promising results on initial imaging but the 
set arrived so late in the process that it was decided that the data analysis would not be completed 
within the time frame available for the completion of this thesis. Therefore, the focus of the thesis 
==PAGE:69==
Discussion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 67 of 102 
25.06.2015 
shifted to analysis of fibroblast – endothelial co-cultures and the automated imaging and analysis of 
the monocytes must be completed at a later time by another member of the BIG research group. 
==PAGE:70==
Conclusion 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 68 of 102 
25.06.2015 
6. Conclusion 
This study set out to show how topographical gradients in conjunction with image cytometry and 
high throughput automated image analysis software can be used to extract information and provide 
meaningful information on cell behaviour.  
We used fluorescence microscopy in conjunction with the open source cell segmentation software 
CellProfiler to extract information on the actin structure and cell nuclei of endothelial cells and 
fibroblasts. It was found that fibroblasts and endothelial cells were highly heterogeneous when 
cultured on nanoscale gradients. However, it was still possible to classify them by type using a 
random forest classifier with an error rate of just 16.72% based solely on information on actin 
structure.  
We found that substrates with gradients of increasing nano-pillar height from 0 to 250 nm can 
effectively be used as a tool to monitor pillar height influence on cell behaviour on a continuous 
scale. It is believed that this continuous scale could be used as an exploratory tool and the trends 
seen can provide the foundation for choice of which discrete substrates with homogenous pillar 
height should be examined in future studies. It is clear that improvement can be made to the 
suggested procedure to reduce the variance between observations. In particular illumination 
correction and a segmentation algorithm that does not exclude cells at the edge of the image scans 
could potentially remove some variance.  
Inspecting the gradients, it was found that either the fibroblasts actively try to remove themselves 
from nano pillars higher than 150 nm or they fail to proliferate. This trend was not seen in 
endothelial cells. Fibroblast invasion and lack of endothelial cells can cause increased risk of stent 
restenosis. Therefore, substrates with pillar height in the 150-250 nm range are suggested for future 
studies in biomaterials useable for cardiovascular stents.  
Finally, a collaboration with a laboratory showed that it can be difficult for laboratories 
inexperienced in culturing cells on nanoscale topographies to provide samples of sufficient quality 
to be used in high throughput image analysis.

Appendix A: Features

Appendix A: Features 
Available features 
 
CellProfiler extracts features from identified objects (e.g. nuclei and cells). This chapter contains a 
run-through of all the features extracted from cell objects in this thesis. 
 
Area-Shape Features. 
Area and shape features are the most basic image moments. They are the characteristics that can be 
considered the most obvious geometrical features of an object such as area, perimeter, eccentricity, 
orientation etc. They are the moments most commonly observed by manual visual cell analysis and 
therefore many of these are used and reported in works involving cell morphology. Because the 
features are derived directly from geometric measurements they have the advantage of being easily 
interpretable and have low computational cost. 
 
Area 
 
The number of pixels in the object.  
Perimeter 
 
The number of pixels along the boundary of the object. 
FormFactor:   
!∗#∗$%&'
(&%)*&+&%, 
A measure of circularity as perfectly circular object will have formfactor 
1 
Solidity:  
 The fraction of the pixels in the object relative to the number of pixels in 
the smallest convexed envelope surrounding it. Will be 1 for an object 
without holes and with a convex shape. Wil be less than one for objects 
with ruffled border or internal holes. Se Figure A1. 
 
Figur A1 Example of cell outline with convex envelope. Ruffled cell outline will have solidity below 1. 
Extent:  
The fraction pixels in the object relative to the number of pixels in the

S.S.Lillelund 
Page 76 of 102 
25.06.2015 
smallest rectangle covering the object (bounding box) 
Center_X, Center_Y:  
The x- and y-coordinates of the point farthest from any object edge 
pixel.  
Eccentricity:  
 A measure of elongation of the cell. Distance from center to focus 
devided by the length og the major axis in ellipse with the same second 
moment as the object. Eccentricity of a circle and a line is 0 and 1 
respectively.  
 
MajorAxisLength:  
The length in pixels of the major axis of an ellipse with same normalized 
second central moments as the object.  
MinorAxisLength:  
The length in pixels of the minor axis of an ellipse with same normalized 
second central moments as the object.  
Orientation:  
 The angle in degrees between the x-axis and the major axis of an ellipse 
with the same second-moments as the object.  
Compactness:  
The variance of the radial distance from the centroid of the object to the 
object's pixels devided by the object area.  
 
 
==PAGE:79==
Appendix A: Features 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 77 of 102 
25.06.2015 
Neighbour Features 
Neighbour features are concerned with how objects relate to each other within the spatial domain of 
a sample. These measurements involve measurements such as distance to neighbours, difference in 
orientation and percent of cells touching. Thereby the neighbour measures also provide easily 
interpretable measurements. The advantages of these measurements are that they explain how the 
objects relate to each other in the case of cell and that they might show if cells favour or avoid 
contact, if they spread out or stay in clusters and if the organize themselves in systematic patterns. 
 
NumberOfNeighbors 
Number of objects touching the current object. 
PercentTouching:  
Fraction of the of the object's boundary pixels that is in contact with 
boundary pixels of neighbouring objects. 
FirstClosestObjectNumber: Indentification number of the closest object. 
FirstClosestDistance:  
Distance in pixels to the closest object. 
SecondClosestObjectNumber: Indentification of the second closest object. 
SecondClosestDistance:  Distance to the second closest object. 
AngleBetweenNeighbors: The angle formed with the object center as the vertex and the first and  
second closest object centers along the vectors.  
 
 
==PAGE:80==
Appendix A: Features 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 78 of 102 
25.06.2015 
Intensity Features. 
The intensity features are concerned with pixel intensity. The intensity features calculate summary 
statistics for the pixels in the objects interior and the pixels along the perimeter of objects. In 
addition to summary statistics, it also locates centre of mass for pixel intensity. The advantage of 
using intensity measures on cell images is that it can monitor how a particular organelles stained 
with the fluorescent markers who’s channel is being measured change both in concentration and 
location. In general, an increase in intensity can be interpreted as an upregulation of a particular 
marker or increased density of markers while a change in centre of mass can be interpreted as a 
restructuring of the cell interior. 
However, care must be taken when dealing with intensity measures. They are very sensitive to 
changes in stain intensity and homogenous image illumination. 
By default, CellProfiler rescale image intensity from 0 to 1by dividing the mixel intensity in the 
image with the max pixel intensity. 
 
IntegratedIntensity:  
Sum of pixel intensities within an object. 
MeanIntensity:  
Average pixel intensity within an object. 
StdIntensity:  
Standard deviation pixel intensities within an object.  
MaxIntensity:  
Maximum pixel intensity within an object.  
MinIntensity:  
Minimum pixel intensity within an object. 
IntegratedIntensityEdge: Sum of edge pixel intensities of an object. 
MeanIntensityEdge:  
Average edge pixel intensity of an object. 
StdIntensityEdge:  
Standard deviation of the edge pixel intensities of an object.  
MaxIntensityEdge:  
Maximum edge pixel intensity of an object. 
MinIntensityEdge:  
Minimal edge pixel intensity of an object. 
MassDisplacement: 
The distance between the centers of gravity in the grayscale 
representation of the object and the binary representation of the object. 
LowerQuartileIntensity:  Intensity value of the pixel for which 25% of the pixels in the object 
have lower values. 
==PAGE:81==
Appendix A: Features 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 79 of 102 
25.06.2015 
MedianIntensity:  
Median intensity value of pixels within the object 
UpperQuartileIntensity:  The intensity value of the pixel for which 75% of the pixels in the object 
have lower values.

Appendix A: Features

Radial Distribution 
Radial Distribution measures the intensity distribution from an objects centre to its boundary within 
a specified number of contours. From a selected starting point, either at the point furthest from any 
edge or at a specified point such as a cell nucleus. After identification of starting point the object is 
divided into N number of contours. The contour size is normalized at a per object basis. Each 
contour follows a fraction of the distance from the centre point as seen in Figure A2. Within each 
image the pixel intensity is quantified in one of three ways. This provides a total of 3 measures for 
each ring. 
 
 
Figure A2 modified from https://github.com/CellProfiler/CellProfiler/issues/1255 
 
FracAtD 
 
Provides the fraction of total intensity within a given ring.  
 
MeanFrac 
 Provides the fraction of total intensity within a given ring, normalized by 
the fraction of pixels within that ring. This is done to compensate for the 
increasing area of the rings. 
 
RadialCV 
 Provides a measure of variation of intensity within a ring. This measure 
differs from the proceeding in that each ring is divided into 8 slices as 
seen in figure A3. Within a slice the intensity divided by the number of 
pixels provides eight values one for each slice. The final measure is then 
==PAGE:83==
Appendix A: Features 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 81 of 102 
25.06.2015 
calculated as the standard deviation of the 8 values divided by the mean 
of the eight values. 
 
 
Figure A3: Schematic representation of ring division into slices. Used in calculation of the RadialCV measure.

25.06.2015 
Textural and shape features. 
Shape and texture are fundamental properties of objects therefore it is important to have effective 
shape and texture descriptors53. When working with descriptive textural features it is important to 
find features that are invariant of position size and orientation. Many such moments have been 
proposed Fourier descriptors, autoregressive models, circular harmonic expansions56. CellProfiler 
suggests two sets of shape and texture descriptors. Zernike moments and Haralick features. Because 
these are not as easily interpretable as the proceeding moments and their calculation not as straight 
forward a thorough description will follow.  
 
Zernike 
While the proposed moments are invariant of position, size and orientation they do not have an 
orthogonal basis this implies that correlation exist between the extracted features and thereby 
redundant information is present. In addition, solving the equations to extract moments requires 
solving systems of coupled equations and because of the dependence moments will change if higher 
order terms are added. 
 
Zernike features are measures of shape and texture of an image. Zernike features are a type of 
image moments estimated by estimating the coefficients to Zernike polynomials. 
Zernike polynomials are a set of polynomials that are all perpendicular to the unit disc. It is well 
known that any periodic function can be decomposed into an infinite sum of its sinusoid 
components as done with a Fourier transform. A Zernike transform relies on the same principle as 
any sufficiently smooth phase field can be decomposed into a sum of mutually orthogonal Zernike 
polynomials. 
 
Like with a Fourier Transform the coefficients of each Zernike polynomial can be estimated by the 
Zernike transform. By estimating the coefficients of the Zernike polynomials the shape and texture 
of a cell can be described by the coefficients to the Zernike polynomials. While the entire texture of 
the image would take an infinite sum of Zernike polynomials to accurately describe each 
consequent order or polynomiums provide less and less information. In fact Teh and Chin found 
that higher order Zernike moments captures more details but are susequently also more subsceptible 
to noise57. Therefore, a balance must be found that includes enough information without including 
too much noise. Zernike features also have the advantage working well with greyscale images. 
==PAGE:85==
Appendix A: Features 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 83 of 102 
25.06.2015 
 
Teague was the first to suggest using orthogonal polynomials as basis for image moments53. 
Zernike moments are based on orthogonal polynomials and therefore do not contain redundant 
information and require solving uncoupled equations to estimate moments this also implies that 
inducing higher order terms does not change lower order coefficients.  
 
Zernike moments are not rotation and scale invariant. However, the can easily be normalized56. A 
Zernike moment merely experience phase shift upon rotation therefore the magnitude of the Zernike 
moments remain constant. In addition, normalizing the first Zernike moments with a constant 
renders the moments size invariant. All coefficients to Zernike moments are complex and both 
conjugates are coefficients to the equations. This provides an additional advantage as making the 
moments rotation invariant by taking the magnitude cuts the number of features in half while 
providing the same shape and textural information. The advantage of using and orthogonal basis is 
evident as the individual contributions from each polynomial is easily separated and that the 
moments provide a basis for easy reconstruction.  
 
Teh and chin has compared 5 other sets of texture features to Zernike features and found that the 
Zernike features scored best in redundancy and image representation57. Zernike features has been 
used to successfully classify the cells from the different layers of the cornea59. Boland and 
Murphy14 found that they could successfully quantify localisation patterns of organelles within cells 
and use these to identify 7 different cell types. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:86==
Appendix A: Features 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 84 of 102 
25.06.2015 
Haralick 
Haralick features are measures of texture that is concerned with the spatial distribution of grey tones 
in local areas. Measures of grey tone spatial dependence has successfully been used on satellite and 
aerial imagery, X-rays and microscopy and has provided good results in differentiating between 
things as different as cloud types, ground infrastructure types (roads, railways), chest X-rays and 
Cervical cell types. 
The Haralick features are 14 image moments calculated from the co-occurrence matrix. The Co-
occurrence matrix, C, is a symmetric N x N matrix that describes the frequency at which two grey-
scale levels occur as neighbours in the analysed image. Where N is the number of greyscale levels. 
Thereby the entry Cij is equal to the number of times a pixel with greyscale level i is next to a pixel 
with greyscale level j separated by a distance62. An ordinary greyscale image contains hundreds or 
thousands of greyscale levels how many depends on the bit depth of the image and the intensity 
differences of the photographed object. The construction of the co-occurrence matrix follows 3 easy 
steps. First the greyscale intensities of the image to be analysed is quantized into N discrete 
greyscale levels by thresholding. Secondly the image is traversed pixel by pixel either horizontally, 
vertically, diagonally or anti-diagonally and it is noted in the co-occurrence matrix which greyscale 
levels that occurs together as neighbours between each pixel. Thereby the co-occurrence matrix 
becomes a function of angular relationship as well as distance.  
Finally, the co-occurrence matrix is normalized such that the rows sums to one thereby each entry 
Cij describes the probability of greyscale level iis next to greyscale level j in the direction traversed. 
As there are four different ways to traverse the image it is possible to construct four different co-
occurrence matrices from an image. If only one direction is traversed the matrix is not invariant to 
rotation therefore either four co-occurrence matrixes are constructed or one containing the means of 
the co-occurrences.  
In total, 14 Haralick features can be extrapolated from the co-occurrence matrix with the intent of 
summarizing the texture of the image. Each feature is a different measure of how the greyscale 
levels change across the image and thereby descriptors of texture. As an example if the most 
frequent occurrences of greyscale transitions in the co-occurrence matrix are located along the 
diagonal it means that the majority of pixels are located next to pixels of equal or almost equal 
greyscale level therefore the texture must be very homogenous. 13 Haralick features are specified 
and their formulas can be found at: 
(http://murphylab.web.cmu.edu/publications/boland/boland_node26.html). 
 Some of the most used features will be explained in detail below. 
==PAGE:87==
Appendix A: Features 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 85 of 102 
25.06.2015 
Angular Second Momentum 
 
Also known as the uniformity of energy. It is a measure of homogeneity of texture. It is calculated 
as the sum of squared probabilities in the co-occurrence matrix. Second angular momentum is high 
when image pixels are very similar. When pixels are similar it has few grey scale transitions and 
therefore a few high probabilities are present in the co-occurrence matrix and the sum of squares 
will be high62. If the image is inhomogenous only low probabilities will be present in the co-
occurrence matrix and the sum of square will be low60. 
 
Contrast 
 
 
 
Is a measure of local variations in the object. An object with large local transitions in greyscale 
level will have a high Contrast. Large local transitions will cause high frequencies further away 
from the diagonal in the co-occurrence matrix. The contrast formula weights values further away 
from the diagonal higher than the values closer to the diagonal resulting in higher contrast62. If the 
neighboring pixels are very similar the frequencies close to the diagonal in the co-occurence matrix 
will be high and contrast in the image is will be very low.  
 
Correlation: 
Correlation measures the linear dependence of grey levels. In objects with almost constant grey 
tones and noise the linear dependence will be low however if significant striating occurs a linear 
dependence will be found and a high Correlation will be seen(Haralick 1973). 
 
Entropy 
Measures the amount of information in the image. Often used as a measure of how much an image 
can be compressed without loss of information(Kumar 2014) A completely random distribution 
would have very high entropy because it represents chaos. Solid tone image would have an entropy 
value of 0. This feature can be useful to tell if texture can be considered statistically more chaotic 
(gebejes 2013).

Endothelial Fibroblast Co-culture Sample Preparation 
Materials:  
- 
Green CellTracker™ dye (Molecular Probes,UK)  
- 
Fibroblast cell line hTERT-Bj1 
- 
Endothelial cell line LE2 
- 
Uniform Cell Seeder (UCS) 
- 
10% formaldehyde in PBS  
- 
0.1% triton-X in PBS 
- 
1% BSA in PBS 
- 
1% Tween-20 in PBS  
- 
Culture media 
• Fibroblast incubated in DMEM 
i. 71% Dulbecco’s modified Eagles medium (Sigma, UK) 
ii. 17.5 % medium 199 (Sigma,UK) 
iii. 9% Foetal calf serum (Life Technologies, UK) 
iv. 0.9% 100mM sodium pyruvate (Life Technologies) 
• Endothelial incubated in HAMS 
i. 92.4% HAMS F10 medium (Sigma Aldrick, UK) 
ii. 3 % Foetal Bovine Serum 
iii. 2.9% Antibiotic mixture 
iv. 0.8% ITS liquid media supplement (Sigma Aldrich, UK) 
v. 0.9% Sodium bicarbonate (Sigma Aldrich, UK) 
Method: 
1. 
Endothelial cells incubated in 5 uM green CellTracker™ dye for 30 min at 37C as 
specified by manufacturer 
2. 
Endothelial cells Washed three times by spinning down and resuspending in media to 
ensure complete removal of excess dye 
3. 
Combine cultures of fibroblast and endothelial cells 
 
4. 
Cells seeded on substrate using UCS and allowed to settle for 10 min in flow hood to 
prevent fluid movement.  
5. 
Culture vessel moved to incubator and incubated 37C in 5% CO2 atmosphere 
==PAGE:89==
Appendix B: Staining Protocol 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 87 of 102 
25.06.2015 
6. 
After 1 hour 4 mL fresh media was added to each well of UCS 
7. 
Media was changed after 24 h and every 48 h thereafter until termination of incubation 
period 
8. 
Total culture time 96h

Immunoflourescense staining 
Materials:  
- 
PBS (phosphate buffered saline) 
- 
4% formaldehyde in PBS  
- 
0.1% triton-X in PBS 
- 
1% BSA (Bovine serum) in PBS 
- 
1% Tween-20 in PBS  
- 
1:500 goat anti-paxillin in PBS primary antibody (Abcam) 
- 
1:500 phalloidin-rhodamine in PBS secondary antibody (Life Technologies) 
- 
DAPI mounting fluid (Vectashield 
 
Method: 
1. 
Following incubation samples were washed twice in PBS 
2. 
Fixated in 4% paraformaldehyde at 37C for 10 min 
3. 
Rinsed once in PBS 
4. 
Permeabilize cell membrane in 0.1% Triton-X™ in PBS at 4°C for 10 min  
5. 
Incubate substrate in 1% BSA in PBS at 4°C for 1 hour to reduce nonspecific binding. 
6. 
Incubate with primary antibody goat anti-paxillin diluted at 1:500  in PBS at 4°C overnight 
 
7. 
Wash three times with 1% Tween-20 in PBS on shaker set to slow swirl for 5 min  
8. 
Incubate with secondary antibody 1:500 Alexa Fluor® 488 anti-goat in PBS at 4°C for 1 h 
9. 
Wash three times with 1% Tween-20 in PBS on shaker set to slow swirl for 5 min 
10. 
Store in PBS or mount sample by inverting onto size 0 coverslip using mounting medium 
containing DAPI

Monocyte Sample Preparation protocols 
 
Purifying Monocytes from Buffy Coat using MACS (CD14 MicroBeads) 
 
 
Materials: 
 
- 
50ml Falcon Tubes 
- 
30ml Universal containers 
- 
Sterile PBS pH 7.2 
- 
MACS buffer: 0.5% FBS, 2mM EDTA in PBS (pH 7.2) Buffer should be kept cold 
throughout (4˚C). 
- 
IL-4 cytokine 
- 
GM-CSF cytokine 
- 
CD14 microbeads (Miltenyi Biotech) 
- 
24 well plate, tissue culture treated. 
- 
Cold MACS LS or MS columns (pre-chilled 4˚C) 
- 
Cold MACS purple magnet (pre-chilled 4˚C) 
- 
MACS bright yellow separation filter 
- 
MACS multistand 
- 
Trypan blue 
 
Method: 
 
1. 
Chill MACS magnet and column 4˚C; thus to eliminate non-specific binding during 
separation 
 
2. 
Following cell count, centrifuge PBMCs: acc/dec = 3, 350g, 5min (Thermo centrifuge) at 
room temperature. 
 
3. 
Prepare the MACS buffer, all the reagents must be at 4˚C.  
 
• To make 50mL MACS Buffer in 50mL Falcon tube:  
i. Add 250µL FBS  
ii. Add 200µL EDTA (0.5 M) 
iii. Add 49.5mL cold PBS. 
 
4. 
Discard supernatant, for monocyte purification resuspend pellet in cold MACS buffer with 
CD14 beads. 
 
• For every 1 x 107 PBMCs:  
i. Add 10µL of CD14 beads 
ii. Add 80µL of MACS buffer. 
==PAGE:92==
Appendix B: Staining Protocol 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 90 of 102 
25.06.2015 
 
5. 
 Incubate in dark (wrap with aluminium foil), at 4C for 20 minutes. 
 
6. 
Clean MACS stand and purple MACS magnet with 70% EtOH 
 
• Set up column: Place LS column over purple MACS magnet attached to MACS 
stand. 
• Beneath column, place 30mL universal container labelled ‘CD14 negative’. 
 
7. 
Following 20 min incubation, resuspend PBMC with 20 mL of MACS buffet and 
centrifuge at 3 acc/dec, 350, from 5 min (thermos centrifuge) at room temperature. 
 
8. 
Wash column with MACS buffer (LS: 3mL and MS: 500µL). 
 
9. 
Discard supernatant and re-suspend PBMCs in MACS buffer (LS: 4ml and MS: 500µl). 
 
10. 
Place a filter on top of another 50mL Falcon tube and slowly filters PBMCs suspension. 
 
11. 
Then slowly load filtered PBMCs onto the column (wash existing Falcon tube again with 
MACS buffer (LS: 2mL and MS: 500µL) after removing first round of PBMCs + MACS 
buffer and add again to column). 
 
12. 
Wash column 3 times with MACS buffer. CD14 negative cells will pass through into 
universal labelled ‘CD14 negative’ (LS: 3ml and MS: 500µl). 
 
13. 
Collect CD14 positive cells:  
 
• 
Place a new 20mL Falcon tube labelled ‘CD14 positive’ in the tube rack.  
 
• 
Carefully separate the column from the purple MACS magnet and place column into 
the new 20mL Falcon tube.  
 
• 
Add 5mL / 500µl MACS buffer to LS / MS column ensuring ‘CD14 positive’ Falcon 
tube remain beneath column.  
 
• 
Apply plunger to flush MACS buffer through column and collect ‘CD14 positive’ 
PBMC fraction. 
 
14. 
Cells may be passed through another column for further purification if required. 
 
15. 
Perform cell count on CD14 positive fraction, should be 10% of the total PBMC. 
 
==PAGE:93==
Appendix B: Staining Protocol 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 91 of 102 
25.06.2015 
16. 
After isolating monocytes (CD14 positive cells) from PBMC, the suspended in RPMI 
medium – 3x106 cells/500 ul.  
17. 
500 ul of the cell suspension is seeded on the topographies (which are inside 6-well plates) 
18. 
Incubated at 37oC for 60 mins. This is done to allow cells adhere to the chip 
19. 
2.5 ml of RPMI medium is then added to the wells containing the chips 
20. 
The cells are incubated for 6 days at 37oC, 5% CO2.

Phalloidin and DAPI staining protocol 
 
Materials:  
- 
PBS (phosphate buffered saline)  
- 
4% PFA (paraformaldehyde) in PBS  
- 
0.2% triton-X in PBS  
- 
 5% goat serum in PBS  
- 
Methanolic stock solution of Alexa Fluor® 488 Phalloidin (Life Technologies) 
- 
DAPI (4',6-Diamidino-2-Phenylindole) 
Method: 
1. 
Wash cells once with PBS at room temperature.  
2. 
Cells were fixed in 3 ml of 4% Paraformaldehyde/PBS for 10 min at room temperature 
3. 
After the fixing step, they were washed in PBS 2 times, each for 5 mins 
4. 
Permeablizing step: cells were incubated in 3 ml of 0.2% triton x for 20 min 
5. 
After incubation they were washed in PBS 2 times, each for 5 mins 
6. 
Non-specific binding was blocked by incubating cells with 3 ml of 5% normal goat serum 
in PBS for 30 minutes at RT 
7. 
Subsequently the cells were washed with PBS x 3, each for 5 minutes  
8. 
The cells were incubated in 2 ml of a 1:20 dilution of methanolic stock solution of Alexa 
Fluor® 488 Phalloidin - Life Technologies for 20 mins   
9. 
The cells were washed with PBS ×3, each for 5 minutes.  
10. 
Cells were incubated with 3 ml of 0.1 ug/ml DAPI in PBS for 5 minutes. 
11. 
Cells were washed with PBS ×3 for 5 minutes each 
12. 
Cells were embedded in mounting media (Fluorosave), cover slips were put over the cells. 
Sealing was done using nail polish.

Appendix B: Staining Protocol 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 93 of 102 
25.06.2015 
M1 and M2 phenotypic staining protocol 
Materials:  
- 
PBS (phosphate buffer saline)  
- 
4% PFA (paraformaldehyde) in PBS  
- 
0.2% Tween 20 in PBS  
- 
3% BSA and 1% Glycine in PBS 
- 
5% goat serum in PBS  
- 
Mouse anti-human 27E10 Primary Ab (Thermo scientific) - 1:50 dilution in 5% goat serum 
in PBS 
- 
Rabbit anti-human CD206 Primary Ab (Abcam) – final concentration = 1 µg/ml in 5% 
goat serum in PBS (dilution depends on stock concentration) 
- 
Secondary antibodies - 1:250 dilution of each in 5% goat serum in PBS  
a. 
Rhodamine-x goat anti mouse IgG(H+L) for  mouse 27E10 Primary antibody  
b. Alexa Fluor-488  goat anti rabbit IgG (H+L) for  Rabbit anti-CD206 Primary 
antibody 
- 
DAPI (4',6-Diamidino-2-Phenylindole) - 1:20,000 dilution of stock in PBS, i.e. final 
concentration = 250 ng/ml or 545 nM 
Method:  
1. 
Wash cells once in PBS at room temperature (RT), and fix them with 4 % PFA in PBS for 
10 min at RT.  
2. 
Remove PFA, wash twice with 0.2% Tween 20 in PBS – 5 min at RT for each wash.  
3. 
Add 3% BSA and 1% Glycine in PBS, incubate at RT for 30 min. 
4. 
Wash twice with 0.2% Tween in PBS – 5 min at RT for each wash. 
5. 
Add 5% goat serum in PBS, incubate at RT for 30 min.   
6. 
Add appropriately diluted primary antibody / antibodies, incubate at RT for 1 h.  
7. 
Wash with 0.2% Tween 20 in PBS ×3 – 5 min at RT for each wash. 
8. 
Add appropriately diluted secondary antibody / antibodies, incubate at RT for 1 h in the 
dark. 
9. 
Wash with 0.2% Tween 20 in PBS ×3 – 5 min at RT for each wash. 
10. 
Incubate with appropriately diluted DAPI solution for 5 min at RT.  
11. 
Wash with PBS ×3 – 5 min at RT for each wash. 
12. 
Embed the cells in fluorsave anti-fade medium.  
13. 
Add mounting media (fluromount) and mount coverslips onto slides. 
==PAGE:96==
Appendix B: Staining Protocol 
S.S.Lillelund 
Page 94 of 102 
25.06.2015 
14. 
Seal the edges of the coverslips with nail polish. Store samples in the fridge and image 
ASAP (within one week).
